## **CHAPTER 1**

The city breathes in wet, concrete gasps. Rain from two hours ago evaporates off the asphalt in visible, greasy tendrils, making the whole block look like it’s sweating. My phone buzzes against my thigh, a persistent, angry insect. I know who it is without looking. Ma. The third call. The one that means *I’m not asking anymore, I’m telling*. I let it go to voicemail, the vibration dying into a dull hum that gets swallowed by the bass line thumping from Big Leon’s speaker setup in his open garage three doors down.

I’m on the stoop of 471, fingers tracing the cool, gritty iron of the railing. Watching. Ms. Clara from 469 is arguing with a delivery bot, her arms folded tight across her housecoat. The bot’s single, placid blue eye blinks at her, unmoved. “It says *front door*,” she’s insisting, voice sharp enough to cut glass. “This is the front door. Not the sidewalk. My knees are seventy-two years old, you think I’m coming down there?” The bot repeats its pre-programmed line about delivery zones in a smooth, gender-neutral tone. This is the most interesting thing that’s happened all afternoon.

Another buzz. Not a call this time. A text. I slide the phone out.

**Ma:** *Store. Now. Milk. The rent check from the Millers. And if you see your brother tell him his phone is a brick for a reason.*

I thumb back a single letter: *K.*

Not “okay.” Just *K*. The distance of a single letter feels important. A tiny rebellion measured in keystrokes. I push myself up, the worn soles of my Chucks scraping against the stone step. The air smells like wet brick, fried plantains from somebody’s kitchen, and that faint, ever-present undertone of ozone and garbage.

The walk to Sal’s is four blocks of curated invisibility. Head down, but eyes up. Not making eye contact, but seeing everything. Mr. Gable on the corner, running his usual three-card monte game for the tourists brave enough to stray this far from the boulevard. He gives me a slight nod. I don’t nod back, but I shift my trajectory a fraction, a silent acknowledgment. I see the new graffiti on the shutter of the defunct bodega—a sharp, geometric bird in electric blue, tag of an artist called WISP. Already fading under a layer of buff-gray city paint. Born and erased in the same week.

Sal’s Deli & More is a cave of fluorescent light and condensation-heavy coolers. The bell jangles, a sound older than I am. Sal himself is behind the counter, a mountain of a man scrolling through something on a tablet, thick reading glasses perched on the end of his nose.

“Jas,” he grunts, not looking up.

“Sal.”

“Your mother sent you.”

“How’d you guess?”

“The look on your face. It’s the ‘I’ve been voluntold’ look. Milk in the back. Check’s behind the register.”

I grab the milk, the plastic bottle slick with cold. At the counter, Sal slides a pale yellow envelope across the Formica. *Miller* written in a tight, careful script. He’s still looking at his tablet.

“Anything else? We got new sour straws.”

“I’m good.”

“Suit yourself.” He finally glances up, his eyes magnified huge behind the lenses. “You hear about the thing on the avenue?”

“Which thing?” There’s always a thing.

“The development people. In the suits. They were taking pictures outside the old theater. With those 3D scanner wands. Looked like ghosts trying to commune with the building.”

A cold little stone drops in my gut. The old Avalon. A hulking Art Deco relic, marquee dark for a decade, plaster cherubs crumbling. Our personal ruin. Mine and Eli’s.

“Just pictures?”

“Just pictures. For now.” Sal says it like a warning. He knows what the Avalon is. Knew us when we were small enough to believe the boarded-up ticket booth was a spaceship cockpit. “Tell your mother the corned beef is fresh.”

Outside, the light is starting to go long and golden, that brief city apology between the harsh afternoon and the electric night. I tuck the envelope into the back pocket of my jeans, the paper crinkling against denim. I’m about to turn for home when I see him.

Eli.

He’s halfway down the block, leaning against the chain-link fence surrounding the community garden, talking to a girl I don’t recognize. His laugh cuts through the traffic noise—a short, sharp burst that doesn’t sound like him. Or maybe it’s the new him. He’s wearing a jacket I haven’t seen before, dark green, kinda military surplus. His hair is different, too, cut closer on the sides. He looks older than seventeen. He looks like a sketch of someone.

Ma’s text echoes: *if you see your brother.*

I stand there, holding the milk, caught in the tide of sidewalk traffic. Part of me wants to cross the street, grab him by the arm, deliver the maternal summons. The other part, the bigger part, just watches. He’s animated, talking with his hands, the way he does when he’s explaining one of his projects. The girl is nodding, smiling. She’s pretty. Of course she is.

Then his eyes scan the street and land on me. The animation doesn’t drain from his face, it just… changes. Shifts from open to shuttered. The smile becomes something thinner, a placeholder. He says something to the girl, touches her arm, and starts walking toward me.

“Jasmine,” he says when he’s close enough. He doesn’t call me Jas. Not anymore.

“Elias,” I return. The full-name standoff.

He nods at the milk. “Domestic duties?”

“Someone’s gotta keep the wheels turning. Ma’s looking for you. Your phone’s a brick.”

“It’s charging.”

“It’s been charging for three days.”

He shrugs, a masterpiece of non-commitment. His eyes are the same as mine, dark brown, but where mine feel like they’re always taking in light, his feel like they’re absorbing it, giving nothing back. “What’s up with you?”

“Nothing. Sal says developers are scanning the Avalon.”

That gets a reaction. A flicker in the absorbed eyes. “Scanning for what?”

“What do developers scan for, Eli? To see how fast they can turn it into condos with a juice bar in the lobby.”

He looks past me, down the street toward where the Avalon’s marquee would be visible if the new pharmacy hadn’t blocked the view. “They can’t.”

“They can. They will.”

“Not if it’s landmarked.”

I almost laugh. “Landmarked? By who? The Landmark Committee of Our Childhood Dreams? It’s a rotting building.”

“It’s not rotting,” he says, and his voice is low, intense. “It’s waiting.”

This is the old Eli. The one who believed in the magic of hidden things. The one who spent a whole summer mapping the Avalon’s basement on napkins, convinced there was a sealed-off tunnel to the river. That Eli has been in short supply lately, replaced by this jacket-wearing, strange-girl-talking, phone-dodging phantom.

“It’s waiting for a bulldozer,” I say, but the fight’s gone out of me. “Just go home, okay? She’s making pot roast.”

“Pot roast on a Tuesday. Must be a special occasion.” He digs in his pocket, pulls out a folded twenty, crisp. “Here. For the milk. Or whatever.”

I stare at the bill. “Where’d you get that?”

“I’m working.”

“Doing what?”

“Stuff. For Deacon.”

The cold stone in my gut turns to lead. Deacon. Not a bad guy, not exactly, but not a good one either. He runs a “vintage tech” shop that’s a front for something shadier, deals in data retrieval and “privacy solutions.” Eli’s been fascinated with him since he fixed our ancient media player two years ago.

“Eli, no. Don’t get tangled with him.”

“I’m not tangled. I’m assisting. It’s tech stuff. You wouldn’t get it.” He says it without malice, a simple statement of fact. It stings more that way.

“Right. I just buy the milk.” I don’t take the twenty. “Keep it. Buy your new friend a smoothie.”

His jaw tightens. “Her name is Liza. She’s in my Digital Architecture class.”

“Cool. Tell Liza from Digital Architecture that pot roast waits for no man.”

I turn and walk away, feeling his eyes on my back. I don’t look back. It’s a rule. Looking back gives them power.

By the time I get to our building, the sky is the color of a fresh bruise, purple bleeding into black. The lobby light is flickering again, casting a strobe effect on the cracked tile floor. Our apartment is 4B. Four flights up, no elevator. The stairs smell of lemon cleaner and underlying damp.

I can hear the TV before I even get the key in the lock. The six o’news, the anchor’s voice a practiced drone of traffic accidents and political scandals. I push the door open.

“Finally,” Ma calls from the kitchen. “I was about to send a search party.”

“Got the milk. Got the check.” I kick off my shoes, line them up on the mat.

She appears in the doorway, wiping her hands on a dish towel. My mother is a small woman who occupies space like a much larger one. It’s in the set of her shoulders, the directness of her gaze. She’s still in her work scrubs, pale blue, her hair braided tightly back.

“And your brother?”

“Saw him. He’s coming.”

She reads my face like it’s one of her medical charts. “Where?”

“By Sal’s. Talking to a girl.”

“A girl.” She processes this, one less thing to worry about, one new thing to file away. “Good. Maybe she’ll civilize him. Was she nice?”

“I didn’t do a background check, Ma. She seemed fine.”

“Fine.” She turns back to the kitchen. “Set the table. And put your phone on the charger. The battery anxiety is giving me a rash.”

I do as I’m told. The table is a small, square thing in the nook of the living room. I lay out three plates, three forks, three knives. The ritual of it. The three of us. It feels precarious, like one strong wind could blow the third setting right off the edge.

The pot roast is tender, falling apart. The news segues into weather. Eli slips in just as we’re sitting down, bringing a draft of outside air with him.

“Sorry,” he mumbles, sliding into his chair.

Ma gives him The Look. “Your phone.”

“It lives. See?” He holds it up, screen glowing.

“It’s a miracle,” I say, spearing a carrot.

We eat in a silence that’s not quite comfortable, not quite tense. The clink of cutlery, the chew of meat. Ma asks Eli about school. He gives one-word answers. She turns to me.

“Ms. Dillard called today.”

I freeze. “Why?”

“She says you have ‘immense potential’ that is being ‘systematically underutilized.’ Her words, not mine. She wants you to apply for the summer writing intensive at the university. Full scholarship.”

“I’m working at the rec center this summer. It’s already set.”

“This is a better opportunity, Jasmine.”

“It’s a bunch of rich kids talking about their feelings in sonnets. No thanks.”

“It’s a pipeline. It’s a way out.”

“Out of what?” The words come out sharper than I intended.

Ma puts her fork down. “Out of having to take a rent check from the Millers every month. Out of worrying if Sal’s is going to get bought by a chain. Out.”

Eli is watching us, his eyes darting back and forth like he’s at a tennis match.

“I like the rec center,” I say, quieter. “I’m good at it.”

“You’re good at a lot of things you don’t do,” she says, and it’s not an accusation, just a weary fact. “Think about it. That’s all I ask.”

The rest of the meal passes in a truce. We clear the plates. Eli vanishes into our shared bedroom, claiming homework. I help Ma wash up.

Later, in the room, Eli is at his desk, a pair of noise-canceling headphones on, staring at lines of code on his screen. His side of the room is a museum of our past: model rockets, a disassembled robot kit, a framed photo of us as kids on the Avalon’s fire escape, grinning, covered in dust. My side is cleaner. A stack of library books. A notebook.

I curl up on my bed with my own phone. I open the photos. I don’t scroll through the recent ones. I go deep into the archives, years back. I find the picture of the Avalon’ ceiling. Eli had snuck us in through a loose basement grate. We’d lain on our backs on the sticky floor of the balcony, flashlights off, waiting for our eyes to adjust. And there it was: a night sky painted in peeling gold and midnight blue, constellations with tiny light bulbs for stars, most of them dead. A universe in decay. I’d taken a picture. The flash ruined it, turned it into a blurry smear of color, but I kept it.

Eli taps my shoulder. I jump. He’s taken the headphones off.

“What?” I say.

He holds out his phone. On the screen is a civic website, a dense block of text. “Landmark status,” he says. “It’s a process. You need a historical assessment. Photographic evidence. Community impact statements.”

“Eli…”

“We have the evidence. We’ve been documenting that place since we were ten. My maps. Your pictures. We could build the case.”

“We’re not preservationists. We’re kids who broke into a building.”

“We’re the only ones who care,” he says, and his voice is fierce, the old fire back in his eyes. “Deacon knows a guy at the city planning office. He can get us the forms. Show us how to file.”

“And what does Deacon get out of it?”

“Satisfaction. He hates developers more than we do.”

I look at his face, alight with purpose. This is the brother I know. The one who sees a crumbling theater and doesn’t see an end, but a puzzle. A code to crack. I miss him so much it’s a physical ache.

“It’s a long shot,” I say.

“Everything is.”

Outside, a siren wails, climbing the scale then fading. The city’s nightly lullaby. I look from his eager face to the blurry photo of a painted ceiling on my screen. A dead universe.

“Okay,” I hear myself say. “Show me the forms.”

He grins, a real one, and for a second, we’re just us again. Two kids in a shared room, plotting to save a world that exists only for us. He pulls up a document, starts talking about procedural timelines, historical significance criteria.

I nod along, but part of me is already pulling back, watching us from the ceiling. Watching this fragile, hopeful thing we’re building in the dim light of our bedroom. Knowing, with a cold certainty, that it’s already too late. That the scanners are just the beginning. That some things aren’t meant to be saved.

But I let him talk. I let the hope fill the room, sweet and thick as the smell of pot roast. Just for tonight.

---
## **CHAPTER 2**

The forms are a kind of violence. PDFs with tiny, bureaucratic typefaces, asking questions that have no right answers. *Describe the architectural significance. List notable events held at the location. Provide a rationale for preservation in the context of contemporary urban planning goals.*

Contemporary urban planning goals. I read that line three times. It means: *Give us a reason not to make money.*

It’s Saturday. Eli is at Deacon’s, getting a “tutorial” on the submission process. I’m at the library, because the Wi-Fi is free and the silence feels like a physical substance, something I can push against. My usual spot is in the back, near the periodicals that no one reads anymore, the plastic chairs worn shiny by generations of asses seeking quiet.

I have my notebook open. Not for the forms, but for me. Ms. Dillard says writing is “active seeing.” I think it’s more like archaeology. You brush away the top layer of what happened to find the shape of what it meant.

*Today’s excavation,* I write. *Subject: The Miller Check. Pale yellow envelope. Paper feels like dried skin. The handwriting is Mrs. Miller’s—tight, controlled, each letter a fence post. The money inside isn’t charity. It’s a transaction. Their guilt for our silence. We cash it every month. We are quiet. This is the contract.*

I close the notebook. It doesn’t help.

My phone lights up with a message. Not Eli. An unknown number.

**Unknown:** *Jas? It’s Liza. Eli gave me your digits. Hope that’s cool.*

I stare at the screen. Eli gave her my number. A boundary crossed without a passport.

**Me:** *Hey. What’s up?*

Three dots appear. Dance. Disappear. Reappear.

**Liza:** *He talks about you a lot. Says you’re the brains of the operation.*

**Me:** *What operation?*

**Liza:** *The Avalon thing. It’s kinda beautiful. Quixotic.*

Quixotic. A ten-dollar word. A Digital Architecture word.

**Me:** *It’s a thing he’s into.*

**Liza:** *We’re all into things. Wanna see something?*

A link appears. I hesitate, then tap it. It opens to a private gallery. 3D renderings. At first, I don’t understand what I’m looking at. It’s the Avalon, but not. The crumbling facade is cleaned, restored, the marquee blazing with light. But it’s not a theater anymore. The interior is gutted, replaced with open floors, floating staircases, people working at sleek desks. A co-working space. A café where the orchestra pit used to be. The painted ceiling is gone, replaced by exposed ductwork and track lighting.

My stomach turns over.

**Me:** *What is this?*

**Liza:** *My final project. Adaptive reuse. Taking a non-viable historical asset and repurposing it for community-centric, revenue-positive urban integration.*

I read the text twice. Each word is a little pill of poison.

**Me:** *You’re turning it into an office.*

**Liza:** *A collaborative hub. With a public gallery space. The facade is preserved! It’s a realistic solution. The kind developers actually go for. I showed Eli. He thinks it has merit.*

The library air feels suddenly thick, hard to pull into my lungs. Eli thinks it has merit. He saw these images, these sterile, gutted images, and he didn’t vomit. He found *merit*.

**Me:** *Gotta go.*

I shove the phone into my pocket. It feels radioactive. I gather my things, the forms, my notebook, my pen. My hands are trembling. It’s stupid. It’s just a building. A rotting building.

I walk without destination. The day is bright, cold, the sky a hard, clean blue. I end up outside the Avalon without deciding to go there. I just… arrive.

It looks smaller in the daylight. More pathetic. The marquee is a skeleton, rusty iron bones. The posters behind glass are faded to pink and gray ghosts. One cherub has lost its head.

Sal was right. There are marks. Fresh ones. Little spray-painted arrows and numbers on the sidewalk, the kind surveyors use. A bright orange sticker on the boarded-up main door: *STRUCTURAL ASSESSMENT IN PROGRESS. NO UNAUTHORIZED ENTRY.*

I circle the block, to the alley on the west side. Our entrance. The loose grate. It’s still there, but someone has piled black bags of restaurant garbage in front of it. The smell is ripe, sweetly rotten.

“They’re making it harder.”

I spin around. Deacon is leaning in the back doorway of his shop, two storefronts down. He’s a tall, lean man with a shaved head and a perpetual five-o’clock shadow. He wears a vintage bowling shirt and looks like he hasn’t slept in a week.

“Making what harder?” I ask, trying to keep my voice level.

“Access.” He pushes off the doorframe and walks toward me, his steps silent on the cracked asphalt. He nods at the garbage bags. “That’s new. A deterrent. Not very subtle.” He looks at me, his eyes a pale, watery blue. “Eli says you’re on board with the landmark push.”

“He says you know a guy.”

“I know several guys. And women. The city is a machine, Jasmine. It runs on favors and filings. You need to learn which levers to pull.”

“And you’re going to teach us.”

“I’m going to point you at the levers. What you do with them is your business.” He lights a cigarette, the smoke curling into the cold air. “But you should know. It’s not just developers. There’s a community board meeting next Thursday. The Avalon is on the agenda. ‘Discussion of potential for city-led revitalization.’ That’s the first step. They get the neighborhood to agree it’s a ‘blight,’ then they can fast-track the demolition. Your landmark application would… complicate that.”

“So we need to file before Thursday.”

“You need to *start*. And you need to show up. Voices in a room matter more than words on a form. Especially voices that live here.”

I look from him to the Avalon, to the garbage blocking our grate. “Why are you helping?”

He takes a long drag. “I like old things. They have history. You can’t debug a system if you don’t know its original code. This city…” He gestures vaguely with the cigarette. “It’s overwriting its own code. Bad practice. Leads to crashes.”

It’s the most Eli thing I’ve ever heard Deacon say. No wonder my brother is drawn to him. They speak the same language of systems and solutions.

“Eli showed you Liza’s designs,” I say. It’s not a question.

Deacon’s expression doesn’t change. “The adaptive reuse model. Yes.”

“And?”

“And it’s a good student project. It’s not preservation. It’s taxidermy. You stuff the skin and pose it. The soul is gone.” He flicks ash. “Eli sees the technical cleverness. The compromise. He’s learning to think like that. It’s a useful skill. For surviving.”

“I don’t want to survive like that.”

He almost smiles. “Then you’d better learn to fight like hell the other way. Thursday. Seven PM. Community Center. Be there. Bring your brother. And bring pictures. Not the happy ones. The ones that show the bones. The good bones.”

He turns and walks back to his shop, disappearing into the gloom inside.

I’m left alone with the garbage and the orange sticker and the terrible, hollow feeling that I am standing at a hinge-point. That things are about to swing shut.

When I get home, Eli is already there, at our desk, two screens glowing. On one, lines of code. On the other, Liza’s 3D model of the Avalon, rotating slowly. He’s studying it like a blueprint.

“Deacon says we need to go to the community board meeting on Thursday,” I say, dropping my bag.

He doesn’t turn around. “I know. He texted me. I’m cross-referencing the landmark criteria with the original building specs. If we can prove the interior plasterwork is by that Italian artist everyone likes, it adds points.”

“Eli.”

“Hmm?”

“Look at me.”

He swivels in his chair. His face is lit by the cool glow of the screens. “What?”

I point to the rotating model. “This. This is what they want to do. This is the ‘compromise.’ You get to keep the shell so people can feel good about history while they order a seven-dollar latte.”

“It’s better than a pile of rubble, Jas.”

“Is it? Is a ghost better than a corpse?”

He sighs, runs a hand over his short hair. “You’re being emotional. This isn’t about feelings. It’s about strategy. Liza’s model shows a path to economic viability. That’s a language the board understands. We use it to buy time for the landmark status.”

“We’re not allies with the people who want to gut it!”

“We don’t have the luxury of pure allies!” His voice rises, then he glances at the door, lowers it. “We have to be smart. We have to play the game. Deacon gets that. Why don’t you?”

“Because the game is rigged! And Deacon… I don’t trust him, Eli. What’s he really getting out of this?”

“Maybe he just gives a shit about something other than his own life!”

The words hang in the air, sharp as broken glass. He immediately looks like he wants to take them back, but he doesn’t.

I feel something in me close off, a door slamming shut. “Right,” I say, my voice flat. “I’ll just be over here, not giving a shit. You work on your… strategy.”

I grab my jacket. I need air that isn’t this air.

“Where are you going?”

“Out.”

Ma is in the living room, paying bills on her tablet. She looks up as I storm past. “Jasmine?”

“Library,” I lie. “Group project.”

The street is a relief. The noise, the movement, the impersonal flow of it. I walk. I walk past Sal’s, past the community garden, past the new pharmacy that blocks the view. I walk until my feet hurt and the cold air burns my lungs.

I find myself on the bridge. Not the big, famous one, but the old steel truss bridge that crosses the industrial canal. It’s where you go when you don’t know where else to go. The wind whips up from the black water below, carrying the smell of rust and salt.

I pull out my phone. I open the camera. I don’t take pictures of the water or the skyline. I turn the lens on myself. My face is pale, my eyes look huge and lost. The wind is tearing at my hair. I look like a stranger. I look like someone who is about to lose a fight they only just started to fight.

I don’t take the picture. I lower the phone.

Instead, I open my notebook app. I start typing. Not archaeology. Not now. Now, it’s just a scream into the void.

*He is choosing their language. He is learning to speak in terms of viability and compromise and strategy. He is looking at a picture of our universe gutted and he sees merit. When did that happen? When did the brother who believed in tunnels to the river start believing in collaborative hubs? Was it a slow leak, or did someone just pull the plug?*

*I am standing on a bridge. The city is on one side. The past is on the other. I am in the middle, and the wind is trying to push me both ways at once.*

*I have to go to that meeting on Thursday. I have to stand in a room and use words to fight a machine that eats words for breakfast. I have to look at my brother, who is becoming a stranger, and remember he’s the only ally I’ve got.*

*The water below is very dark. It looks like it could swallow anything.*

I stop typing. I put the phone away. I watch a barge move slowly down the canal, its running lights smearing red and green on the water.

Then I turn and walk back toward the city, toward the light, toward the fight waiting in a fluorescent-lit room. One step at a time. Pulled forward not by hope, but by a stubborn, furious refusal to let the code be overwritten without a goddamn comment.

---
## **CHAPTER 3**

The Community Center on Denton Avenue smells like floor wax and anxiety. It’s a low, brick building from the 1970s, with windows that don’t open and acoustic tiles stained yellow from decades of cigarette smoke, back when you could smoke anywhere. Now, the air is thick with the scent of cheap coffee and damp wool.

We’re late. Ma made us eat. “You can’t fight on an empty stomach,” she’d said, putting plates of eggs and toast in front of us. An uneasy truce had settled over breakfast. Eli and I spoke in practicalities. *Do you have the printed photos? Did you save the PDF to your drive?* The deeper rift, the one about Liza’s models and the nature of compromise, was papered over with logistics.

Now, we slip into the back of the main meeting room. It’s already packed. I see Ms. Clara, still looking furious. I see Mr. Gable, sans his monte cards, looking uncharacteristically serious. I see Sal, arms crossed over his broad chest, leaning against the back wall. He gives me a tight nod.

Up front, on a raised dais, sits the community board. Five people behind a long table draped with a blue cloth. There’s Mrs. Garelli, who’s been on the board since the Pleistocene epoch, her hair a helmet of white. Mr. Chen, who owns the hardware store. Two younger people I don’t recognize. And in the center, a man in a trim navy suit. He doesn’t look like he belongs here. He looks like he beamed in from a different planet—one with better tailoring and colder sunlight.

The suit is talking. His voice is smooth, amplified by a tinny microphone. “…a unique opportunity for sustainable growth and neighborhood integration. The Avalon site has been a persistent challenge, a drain on community resources and safety.”

Eli stiffens beside me. I feel my own hands curl into fists in my pockets.

“Our proposal,” the suit continues, “respects the historical character while embracing a forward-looking vision. A mixed-use development featuring affordable housing units, retail space anchored by a national pharmacy, and a state-of-the-art community technology hub.”

A low murmur rolls through the room. *Affordable housing* gets a few nods. *National pharmacy* gets some grimaces. *Technology hub* is met with blank stares.

Mrs. Garelli leans into her mic. “Mr. Vance, the floor is now open for community comments. We’ll keep it to two minutes per speaker.”

A line forms at a microphone stand in the aisle. The first speaker is an older man who complains about parking. The second is a woman worried about construction noise. It’s all the usual small change of neighborhood politics. The Avalon itself is just the empty lot at the center of their concerns.

Then Liza steps up to the mic.

I feel Eli go perfectly still. She’s dressed differently than she was on the street—dark trousers, a simple sweater, her hair pulled back. She looks professional. Credible.

“Hello,” she says, her voice clear. “My name is Liza Cho. I’m a student in Urban Design at the university, and a resident of the neighboring district. For my senior thesis, I’ve been studying the Avalon as a case study in adaptive reuse.” She holds up a tablet, turns it to show the board. Even from the back, I can see the familiar, gutted interior. My throat tightens.

“As you can see, it’s possible to preserve the iconic facade—the marquee, the terracotta details—while transforming the interior into a vibrant, revenue-positive asset. A co-working space, a public café, a gallery for local artists. This model increases foot traffic, supports small businesses, and provides a modern resource without erasing our history.”

She’s good. She’s really good. She’s speaking their language, but with a youthful, idealistic spin. I see Mr. Chen nodding. The suit, Mr. Vance, is smiling a thin, approving smile.

“Thank you, Ms. Cho,” Mrs. Garelli says. “A very thoughtful presentation. Next?”

Eli is already moving. He steps into the aisle and walks to the mic. He didn’t tell me he was going to speak. My heart hammers against my ribs.

“Hi,” he says, his voice slightly shaky at first. He clears his throat. “I’m Elias Ward. I live at 471. I’ve lived here my whole life.” He holds up a thick folder. “The Avalon isn’t just a building to me. To a lot of us. It’s… it’s part of the neighborhood’s original code. We’ve started the process to file for official landmark designation.” He pulls out a photo, one of mine, printed on good paper. It’s a close-up of the painted ceiling, the gold leaf and the faded blue, one intact star-bulb glowing like a distant sun. He passes it to Mrs. Garelli. “This is what’s inside. This is the history worth saving. Not just the facade. The soul. Landmark status would protect all of it. Forever.”

He sounds young. Passionate. He sounds like my brother. Mr. Vance’s smile has vanished.

“Landmark status is a lengthy, uncertain process, young man,” Mr. Vance says, interjecting before the board can respond. “During which the building continues to decay, becoming a greater safety hazard. Our proposal is ready now. It provides housing. Jobs.”

“It provides you a profit,” a new voice says, loud and clear from the back.

All heads turn. Deacon is standing near the door, having slipped in unnoticed. He’s not at the mic, but his voice carries. He’s still in a bowling shirt. He looks utterly out of place and completely unfazed. “Your ‘technology hub’ is a franchise. Your ‘affordable housing’ starts at rents double what most people on this block pay. You’re not integrating the neighborhood. You’re pricing it out. You’re rebranding displacement.”

The room erupts. Someone shouts, “Tell him!” Mrs. Garelli bangs a gavel. “Order! Please, one speaker at a time!”

Mr. Vance’s face is tight. “And you are?”

“A concerned citizen. And a business owner who actually pays taxes here. That kid—” he points at Eli, “—and his sister have more genuine stake in this place than your whole corporate portfolio. Landmark the building. Let the community decide what happens to it *after* it’s saved from the wrecking ball. Not before.”

It’s a direct, brutal counter-punch. The room is buzzing. I see Sal grinning. I see Ms. Clara nodding fiercely.

Mrs. Garelli bangs the gavel again. “The board will take all comments under advisement. The matter of the Avalon is tabled for further review. Thank you all.”

The meeting is breaking up. People are clustering, talking loudly. Eli is surrounded by a few older residents, shaking his hand, clapping him on the back. He’s flushed, smiling. Liza is talking to Mr. Chen, her tablet still in hand. She glances over at Eli, her expression unreadable.

Deacon catches my eye from across the room. He gives me a slow, deliberate nod. Then he turns and melts back out the door.

I feel unmoored. It was a skirmish, not a battle won, but something shifted in the room. Eli’s passion, Deacon’s harsh truth—it made Liza’ sleek presentation feel like what it was: a pretty package for the same old product.

Eli finds me as the crowd thins. His eyes are bright. “Did you hear? They listened!”

“They heard,” I correct. “Listening is different.”

“It



---

# Chapter 4

## **CHAPTER 4**

The rain came back. Not the heavy, honest downpour of the afternoon, but a fine, whispering mist that blurred the streetlights into smears of jaundiced gold. It beaded on the shoulders of my jacket, a cold, persistent weight. I followed the woman—Elara—because the alternative was standing in that emptying community center hallway, feeling the after-buzz of the meeting dissolve into the usual, hollow static. Deacon was gone. Eli was riding a high that would crash by morning. Liza was probably already in a town car, drafting a damage report on her tablet. The city was breathing its wet, concrete breath, and I had nowhere to exhale.

Elara moved like she was part of the mist. She didn’t look back, just assumed I’d follow the straight line of her spine, the dark wool of her coat swallowing the weak light. We cut through an alley that smelled of damp brick and rotting herbs, past the shuttered back doors of restaurants. The music from Big Leon’s speakers was a muffled, dying heartbeat behind us.

“It’s not far,” she said, her voice the same low, frayed instrument from the hallway. It wasn’t reassurance. It was a fact.

She stopped at a rusted service door set below street level, beside a dumpster humming with flies. The key she pulled from her pocket wasn’t the one from the box. It was modern, steel. But the way she held it—a specific, deliberate pressure between thumb and forefinger—echoed something. She didn’t just turn the lock. She paused, her head tilted as if listening to the tumblers, then pushed the door open on a sigh of damp air.

The space inside was a vault of shadows and forgotten things. A boiler room, maybe, once. Now it was a cave. The only light came from a single, shaded lamp on a worktable, its glow pooling on schematics that weren’t blueprints, on tools that didn’t look like they were for fixing pipes. The walls were lined with shelves holding what looked like junk: broken clock parts, tarnished brass fittings, geodes split open to reveal crystalline teeth. It smelled of ozone, old paper, and the sharp, clean scent of solder.

“Sit,” she said, nodding to a stool by the table. She shrugged off her coat, revealing a sweater the color of charcoal. Her movements were economical, without waste.

I stayed standing, my back to a cold concrete pillar. “You knew my mother.”

“I knew *of* her.” Elara lit a small gas ring under a kettle. The blue flame hissed, a tiny, fierce sun in the gloom. “She was a keeper. A guardian. One of the last who understood the door wasn’t just a door.”

“The Avalon.” The name felt different here. Not a crumbling building, but a weight.

“A shell. A keystone.” She poured hot water into two chipped mugs. “Your city and another… they grew up twined. Like two trees sharing the same root. The Avalon sits on a place where the bark is thin. Where you can, if you know how, step from one trunk to the other.”

I thought of the feeling in the lobby. The pressure, the hum. The way the dust didn’t settle right. “Another city.”

“Another *world*,” she corrected, placing a mug in front of me. The tea inside was black, unsweetened. “A reflection. A twin. It was a place of exchange. Of balance. The guardians—your bloodline—maintained the door. They curated what passed through: ideas, art, whispers of technology. Nothing that would break the balance. It was a conversation. A long, slow, beautiful conversation between two worlds.”

She took a sip of her tea, her eyes on the steam. “Then The Silence came.”

The name landed in the quiet room like a stone in deep water. It didn’t need explanation. The way she said it—the capital letters audible in her quiet voice—was enough.

“They are not from the other side. They are a… force. A negation. They don’t conquer. They erase. They smooth over the unique, the specific, the remembered. In their world, they turned cities into perfect, repeating grids. No history. No memory. Just efficient, silent function. They consumed their reflection. Now they want ours.”

I saw it then, with a cold clarity. Liza Vance’s presentation. The sleek, generic tech hub. The affordable, identical housing units. It wasn’t just gentrification. It was a blueprint. The first pass of an eraser.

“They work through people here,” I said, my voice rough. “People who want things clean. Simple. Quiet.”

Elara nodded. “They offer order. A terrible, peaceful order. They find the cracks—greed, fear, a desire to forget—and they widen them. The Avalon is the last true thin place. If they demolish it, build their seamless, silent architecture on top… the door closes forever. Or worse, it becomes a one-way chute. Everything that makes this place *this place* gets slowly drained into their void.”

She set her mug down and looked at me, really looked. Her eyes were the grey of old ice. “You are a descendant of the guardians. The blood is thin, the memory is faint, but it is there. It’s why the key woke for you. It’s why you feel the hum in the walls. You are a thread to what was. The last thread.”

I laughed, a short, dry sound that bounced off the concrete. “I’m a guy who fixes old stereos and dodges his mother’s calls. I’m not a guardian of anything.”

“Memory isn’t about grand recall,” she said, her voice dropping. “It’s in the muscle. In the gut. It’s the way you listen to a broken circuit, not just for the break, but for the *sound* of the break. It’s the way you trace a seam in the iron railing on your stoop. You are looking for the fit. The true fit. That is the essence of the key.”

She reached into a drawer and pulled out a small, felt-wrapped bundle. Unfolding it, she revealed the key from the box. In the lamplight, it seemed to drink the glow, holding it in its intricate grooves.

“It doesn’t just open physical locks,” she said, holding it out to me. “It responds to intent. To memory. Try.”

I didn’t take it. “Try what? There’s no door here.”

“Every thing that is made holds the memory of its making. Of its purpose.” She gestured to a small, battered metal cabinet in the corner. A simple padlock held it shut. “That lock is a memory of security. A thought given form. Feel for the thought. Then turn.”

Skepticism was a coat I wore tight. But under it, curiosity was a live wire. I took the key. It was warmer than I expected. I walked to the cabinet, knelt. The padlock was cold, impersonal. I inserted the key. It fit, but it was inert. Just metal in metal.

“Stop looking with your eyes,” Elara’s voice came from behind me. “Listen.”

I closed my eyes. The hum of the gas ring. The drip of water somewhere. The distant, warped thump of bass. I pushed them back. I thought of the lock. Not its shape, but its *idea*. A barrier. A promise that what was inside would stay inside. I thought of the click it wanted to make, the smooth surrender of its mechanism when the right truth was told to it.

In my hand, the key grew warmer. Not hot, but alive. A vibration, subtle as a cat’s purr, traveled up my arm. I didn’t turn it. I *suggested*. I offered the idea of *open*.

The lock clicked. A soft, definitive sound.

I opened my eyes. The shackle had sprung. My breath caught in my throat. It wasn’t magic. It was… resonance. Like tuning a string to the exact frequency to shatter a glass.

“The Silence works by severing those memories,” Elara said. “By making a place so bland, so without history, that it holds no resonance. Nothing for the key to listen to. A door in a white room is just a wall.”

I stood, the key heavy in my palm. The thrill of the click was already fading, replaced by a deeper dread. The scope of it was too vast. “I can’t fight a… a force. With a skeleton key.”

“You don’t fight them head-on. No one can. You remember. You preserve the specific, the irregular, the true. You anchor the memory of this place so deeply that their erasure can’t take hold. The Avalon is the heart. You have to protect the heart.” She moved to the worktable, lifted a floorboard beneath it. From the hidden space, she pulled out a book.

It was a journal, bound in leather so old it was nearly black. It was thick, bulging with inserted papers, maps, loose sketches. She handled it with a reverence that was physical.

“This belonged to Alastor Vale. Your great-great-grandfather. The last full guardian.” She placed it in my hands. It was surprisingly light, like held ash. “He recorded everything. Maps of the twin city. The principles of the door. The early signs of The Silence. And his warnings.”

I opened it carefully. The pages were brittle, covered in a tight, precise script. Sketches of the Avalon’s foundation, but with layers beneath it—a ghostly, inverted architecture. Diagrams of the key. And on one page, a single phrase, underlined heavily: *They do not come with shouts, but with shushes. They make you forget what you are fighting for.*

“This is your inheritance,” Elara whispered. “Not property. Not a title. A responsibility. To remember.”

I was tracing the lines of a map—our neighborhood, but not, the streets warped into elegant, impossible spirals—when the world outside went quiet.

The bass from Big Leon’s stopped. Not fading. Cutting off mid-beat.

The hair on my arms stood up. Elara went still, her head cocked. It wasn’t silence. It was a vacuum. A *listening* silence.

“They’re here,” she said, her voice flat. She blew out the lamp, plunging us into absolute black. “The journal. The key. Go. Out the back.”

“How did they find—”

“They track resonance. The click of the lock. A guardian using the key… it’s a flare in the dark to them.” She was a shadow, moving swiftly, gathering nothing. “Go. Now.”

A sound from the alley above. Not footsteps. More like a soft, steady *brush* against the asphalt. Like something being smoothed over.

My heart was a frantic drum against my ribs. I shoved the journal inside my jacket, the key clenched in my fist. Elara pushed me toward a low, arched opening in the back wall I hadn’t seen—a coal chute, maybe. “Follow the old steam tunnels. They’ll take you to the river. Don’t stop. Don’t look back.”

“What about you?”

Her smile was a faint, grim gleam in the dark. “I’m a memory they’ve already tried to erase. I’m harder to forget the second time.”

The brushing sound was at the service door. No knock. No attempt at the lock. A slow, insistent pressure.

I scrambled into the chute, cold, slimy brick scraping my back. I looked back once. Elara stood facing the door, her hands at her sides, perfectly still. She was a silhouette against the faint grey light from the street grate. A fixed point.

Then I was moving, crawling backward into a tighter, colder darkness. The last thing I heard from the room was the sound of the service door buckling inward, not with a crash, but with a long, weary groan of metal giving up its purpose. There was no shout. No cry. Just that hungry, swallowing silence, rushing in.

And then, nothing.

I ran. The tunnel was a throat of brick and dripping pipes. I didn’t have a light. I ran by feel, by the desperate memory of her words: *to the river*. The journal thumped against my chest with every step. The key was a brand in my sweaty palm.

I thought of Eli’s bright, hopeful face. *They listened!* I thought of Ms. Clara arguing with a delivery bot. Of Deacon’s slow nod. Of the greasy tendrils of evaporating rain. The specific, stubborn, messy truth of my block. My city.

The Silence wanted to smooth it all into a quiet, efficient grid. And I was now, somehow, the ragged, breathing splinter in their plan. A keeper of a memory I was only just beginning to understand.

The tunnel began to slope downward. The air grew colder, smelling of mud and rust. In the distance, I could hear the low, mournful groan of a foghorn on the river.

I stopped, leaning against the wet wall, gasping. I pulled out the journal. I couldn’t see it, but I could feel its weight, its fragile truth. Alastor Vale’s warnings. My inheritance.

Somewhere above, in the sweating, breathing city, a force that erased memories was hunting me. And the only weapon I had was the ability to remember.

The foghorn sounded again, closer now. A guide. Or a dirge.

I pushed off the wall and started moving toward the sound, one foot in front of the other, into the damp, echoing dark.



---

# Chapter 5

## **CHAPTER 5**

The tunnel was a throat, and I was the word it was trying to swallow. My breath came back to me off the wet brick, hot and panicked, a private echo chasing my heels. The foghorn was a distant, wet pulse. The only other sound was the thump of the journal against my ribs and the slick, frantic scuff of my shoes on stone.

I didn’t know how long I’d been running. Time down here was measured in heartbeats and the slow, indifferent drip of water from pipes older than my grandfather. The memory of that door groaning inward played on a loop behind my eyes. Not a crash. A surrender. And Elara, standing in its path, a fixed point until the dark ate her.

My chest hitched. I forced the air in, forced the legs to move. *To the river.*

A new sound threaded itself under my panting. Not the horn. A dry, skittering whisper, like insect legs over paper. It came from behind. And it was keeping pace.

I didn’t look. Looking was for people who had time. I just ran harder, the key a slick, hard knot in my fist. The slope steepened. The air thickened with the smell of wet rot and diesel fuel. The river’s breath.

The whisper grew. Became a rustle. Became the sound of many dry things moving as one. It wasn’t footsteps. It was the sound of forgetting, given legs.

Ahead, the tunnel forked. One path continued sloping down, wide and dark. The other was a narrower pipe, its entrance choked with rusted cables. No sign, no marker. Just a choice.

Elara’s voice in my memory, calm as a stone at the bottom of a well: *When the river hears you, turn toward the oldest smell. Iron and ozone.*

I skidded to a halt, lungs burning. I listened. The mournful horn. The skittering chorus, closer now. And underneath it, a vibration through the soles of my feet—the deep, churning growl of the river’s current. I turned my face. The narrow pipe exhaled a draft that was cold and carried a tang like a struck match. Ozone. And beneath it, the old-blood smell of rust.

I dove into the pipe. It was tighter, my shoulders scraping both sides. I had to crouch, to move in a stumbling, bent-over scramble. The skittering stopped at the entrance. For a second, there was only the thud of my own blood in my ears. Then a single, smooth *click*, like a recorder switching on.

A voice filtered from behind, smooth and genderless, devoid of any breath. It didn’t echo. It was just *there*, inside the pipe with me.

“Alex. Stop running. You’re confused. You’ve had a shock. Let us help you order your thoughts.”

It was the voice from a thousand public service announcements. The voice that told you the recycling schedule, that reminded you to smile for your civic contentment scan. It was poison wrapped in honeycomb.

I kept moving, crawling now on hands and knees through icy runoff.

“Elara Vance is a known mnestic hazard. A corrosive element. Her influence has fractured your recall stability. Come back. Let us smooth the edges.”

I saw her silhouette against the grey light. Perfectly still. A fixed point. My fingers closed around the key. *A splinter*, I thought. *I’m a splinter.*

“Your mother is very worried,” the voice said, and it was the most terrifying thing it had uttered yet, because it used her like a tool, sanding down the specific worry in her eyes into a generic, usable fact.

I found my own voice, rough and broken. “Tell her I’ll call her back.”

I surged forward. The pipe ended abruptly, spilling me out onto a narrow, slick walkway carved into the side of a vast, cylindrical shaft. The sound was immense—the roar of water far below, the shriek of ancient machinery. I was inside a runoff cistern, a cathedral of decay. Above, a single strip of emergency lighting cast a jaundiced glow over moss-slick walls and giant, corroded gears frozen in mid-turn.

And there, directly across the chasm, maybe twenty feet of impossible empty space, was the other side of the walkway. It led to a metal door, rusted shut, set into the curved wall.

The skittering began again in the pipe behind me. They were coming.

This was it. A dead end. A joke. I fumbled for the journal, my numb fingers tearing at the pages. The photograph. I had to *use* it. The key was in my hand, but the door was across a gap I couldn’t jump. My mind raced, a frantic animal. The memory. I had to hold the *specific* memory.

I looked at the photograph. Not at the strange, glowing spires, but at the foreground. At the figure—Alastor?—his hand resting on a railing. The railing was ornate, ironwork twisted into the shape of vines. And in the background, just behind his shoulder, a utility lamp on a curved wall. A wall that curved like this one.

The memory wasn’t of a place. It was of a *feeling* in a place. The feeling of standing right here, the roar of the river below, the smell of iron and ozone, the cold fear, the weight of a choice. The feeling of being on the precipice of a hidden world.

I closed my eyes. I let the present sensations flood in—the damp, the roar, the metallic taste of panic. I overlaid them with the image from the photograph, not as a picture, but as a lived moment. I imagined the heat of that strange sun on my neck, the smell of ozone not from rust, but from the air itself. I focused on the key in my hand, not as a piece of metal, but as a *turn*. A transition.

The voice was at the pipe’s mouth. “There’s nowhere to go, Alex. Let us help you forget this stress.”

I opened my eyes. I looked not at the far walkway, but at the space between. At the solid, empty air over the roaring drop.

And I remembered what it was to cross.

I stepped off the ledge.

There was no lurch, no falling sensation. My foot came down on something solid, but invisible. A vibration hummed up through my bones, a low, crystalline note. I took another step, then another, walking on memory given form. The key in my hand grew warm. With each step, the world around me *wavered*. The cistern flickered—the rusted gears became, for a fraction of a second, intricate sculptures of light. The roar of water became a chorus of distant, chiming bells. It was like tuning a radio, sliding from one station of reality to another, the static of both worlds screaming in my ears.

I was halfway across when I heard the pipe disgorge its contents.

I didn’t turn. I kept my eyes on the door ahead, now shimmering like a heat mirage. The agents of The Silence didn’t make a sound. But I felt their regard, a cold pressure on the back of my neck, a vacuum trying to suck the memory out of my head and leave only the empty, placid script.

I reached the door. It was no longer just rusted metal. It was both things at once: the pitted, red-brown surface, and beneath it, a sheen of pearlescent material, like the inside of a shell. A keyhole glimmered in its center.

The skittering was on the walkway behind me. They were moving fast.

I shoved the key into the lock. It fit perfectly. The warmth from the key flooded into the mechanism. I turned it.

The sound was not a click, but a sigh. A release of held breath that had been kept for a century. The door dissolved. Not opening, but *unfolding*, like a flower made of light and shadow, revealing not the other side of the cistern wall, but a vista that stole the air from my lungs.

I stumbled through.

And the world shattered.

***

The noise ended. All of it—the river’s roar, the skittering pursuit, the hum of the city above. It was cut off as completely as a slamming vault door.

The silence that replaced it was absolute, and heavy, and *different*. It was the silence of a held breath, of a page waiting to be turned.

I stood on a platform of white, veined stone. Before me, a city hung in a twilight sky.

It was the city from the photograph, but the photograph was a lie. It had shown beauty, wonder. It had not shown the decay.

Crystalline spires, miles high, pierced a lavender sky streaked with permanent, frozen sunset. Islands of rock and intricate architecture floated at impossible angles, connected by delicate, gossamer bridges that shimmered with internal light. It was breathtaking. A symphony in stone and light.

But the symphony was out of tune. Great chunks were missing from the spires, their edges jagged, weeping slow trails of glimmering dust. Several of the floating islands listed, their foundations cracked, held aloft by nothing but habit. The gossamer bridges were snapped in places, their severed ends waving like listless tentacles. The light within them pulsed erratically, a sickly, arrhythmic heartbeat.

And the colors. The photograph had been vibrant. This was a faded echo. The crystals were milky, clouded. The lavender sky was bruised at the edges with patches of dull grey, like mold on fruit. The air itself had a sepia tint, as if I were looking at a once-grand painting left too long in the sun.

The smell was ozone, yes, and something sweet like crushed flowers, but under it was the sour tang of neglect, of stagnant water and dust.

I took a step forward, my city shoes crunching on gravel. I turned.

The doorway was gone. In its place was a smooth, seamless wall of the same white stone, carved with faint, spiraling patterns that meant nothing to me. No keyhole. No seam. Just a solid fact.

There was no way back.

The thought landed in my gut like a stone. Ma. Deacon. Ms. Clara and her delivery bot. The stoop at 471. The greasy tendrils of evaporating rain. It was all on the other side of that wall, locked away. The Silence would be smoothing over my absence already. *A runaway. A troubled mind. So sad. Let us help you forget the stress.* I was a memory they were actively erasing.

A sound made me turn back to the broken wonderland. A soft chime, then a scrape.

From behind a crumbled plinth at the edge of the platform, a figure straightened up.

It was Elara. Her clothes were torn at the shoulder, and a fine, glittering dust coated one side of her face. But her eyes were clear, watchful. She held a jagged shard of one of the crystals like a weapon.

“You made the walk,” she said. Her voice was the same, but here, it didn’t sound like a stone in a well. It sounded like the only real thing in this echoing, dying place.

“You’re here,” I said, the words stupid and obvious. “The door… they came in…”

“They got the room. Not me. There are other ways down for those who know how to forget the right walls.” She lowered the crystal shard. “This is it. Lyra. Or what’s left of it.”

I gestured weakly at the crumbling splendor. “It’s… broken.”

“It’s forgotten,” she corrected, her voice sharp. “It’s what happens when something exists only in memory, and the memory starts to fail. When the Rememberers die, or are taken, the things they hold… they fray. They fade.” She looked at me, and for the first time, I saw something raw in her gaze. Not pity. Need. “You felt the key grow warm. You walked the bridge of recall. You’re a Rememberer, Alex. A direct descendant of Alastor Vale. Your blood holds the anchor points for this place. Without you… this goes quiet. Forever.”

The weight of it pressed down, heavier than the strange air. I wasn’t just carrying a journal. I was carrying a world. A dying one.

“Why?” I managed. “Why hide a whole city? Why let it rot?”

“It didn’t rot,” a new voice said, dry and cracked as old parchment.

An old man stepped from the shadow of a leaning archway. He was tall, gaunt, dressed in patched robes that might have once been splendid. His hair was a wild white mane, and his eyes were the cloudy blue of the damaged spires. In his hands, he held a staff of wood that had a single, faintly glowing knot at its top.

“It was wounded,” the old man said, leaning on the staff. “The Silence, in its infancy, made its first major incision here. A trial run. They called it ‘The Great Simplification.’ They couldn’t erase Lyra—it was too real, too tied to the old world’s ley lines. So they severed it. Made it a memory. And memories, if not tended, become dreams. And then… they become nothing.” He bowed his head slightly. “I am Kaelen. The last steward of the Echo Quarter. We have been waiting for Alastor’s heir for a long, long time.”

I looked from his ancient, weary face to Elara’s determined one, to the breathtaking, heartbreaking ruin around me. The journal felt like a lead weight. The key in my pocket was now just a piece of metal. Its job was done.

“What do you need me to do?” I asked, my voice small in the vast, fading silence.

Kaelen’s cloudy eyes seemed to see through me. “First, you must remember something for us. Something we have all forgotten.”

“What?”

“How it begins,” Elara said softly. “The journal. Alastor didn’t just record what The Silence did. He recorded what they *are*. Their origin. Their true name. That memory… it’s locked. Even from us. We have the pieces, but not the key. We think… you are the key.”

Kaelen gestured with his staff toward the heart of the crumbling city. “The Central Spire. The Memory Vault. It is failing. But inside, the core memory of The Silence is held. We cannot reach it. The way is guarded by the Echoes.”

“Echoes?”

“The ghosts of forgotten things,” Kaelen said. “When a memory fades from every living mind, it doesn’t vanish. It… lingers. Hollow. Hungry. They are attracted to new memory, to fresh recall. They will try to drink yours.” He looked at me, and for a moment, the cloudiness cleared, revealing a deep, abiding fear. “To get to the truth, you must walk through a gallery of ghosts. And you must remember your way through. If you forget for even a second… you become one of them. A whisper in the ruins.”

The hook in my chest, the one that had been pulling me since I found the journal, gave a final, decisive tug. This was the shattering. Not a chase, not a hideout. A descent. Into the belly of a forgotten world, to find the memory of the thing that was eating my own.

I looked at my hands. They were just a kid’s hands. Stained with tunnel grime, shaking slightly. The hands that ignored Ma’s calls. The hands that held a dead man’s book.

I thought of Eli. *They listened!* I thought of the stubborn, messy truth of my block. It wasn’t perfect. It was loud and sweaty and arguing with bots. But it was *real*. It wasn’t an echo.

I was the splinter. And a splinter’s job is to dig in.

I met Elara’s eyes. “Okay,” I said.

It wasn’t a brave word. It was just a fact. The next thing to do.

Kaelen nodded, a slow, grave movement. “Then we have no time. The Vault’s light dims with each passing hour. Follow.”

He turned and began to pick his way down a path of broken white stone that wound toward the colossal, listing spires. Elara fell in beside me, her shoulder brushing mine.

“The bridge you walked,” she said quietly as we walked. “The memory you used. What was it?”

I watched my feet on the strange stone. “The feeling of being trapped. Of having a choice that wasn’t really a choice. The feeling right before everything changes.”

She was silent for a long moment. “That’s a good memory. Strong. Hold onto it.”

We walked into the shadow of the first broken spire. The light from the bridges above pulsed weakly, casting long, dancing shadows that seemed to move just at the edge of vision. The air grew colder. From deep within the city of dying crystal, I heard the first sound since the door had sealed.

A whisper.

Not one voice. A thousand. A dry, rustling chorus, sighing words in a language I didn’t know, but understood in the marrow of my bones. It was the sound of loss. The sound of names that no one would ever speak again.

The Echoes knew we were here.

And they were hungry.



---

# Chapter 6

## **CHAPTER 6**

The city didn’t just lie there. It remembered how to stand, and the memory was a stubborn, broken thing. Walking on it was like walking on a dream where the rules kept changing out of spite. The path Kaelen led us down wasn’t flat. It was a spine of shattered white stone that curved up along the side of a fallen tower, turning the world sideways. My left foot was on what felt like ground. My right foot was on what should have been a wall. Gravity was a suggestion here, a weak one.

I focused on the grit under my palms, the cold bite of the crystal. I didn’t look down. Down was a kaleidoscope of other paths, other spires, all intersecting at impossible angles, a geometry drawn by a madman. The air smelled of ozone and old dust, the scent of a television left on in an empty room for decades.

Elara moved like she was part of it, her steps sure, her body leaning into the unnatural slopes as if she’d been born to them. Maybe she had. I moved like a kid who’d had too much sugar, all jerks and corrections. My sneaker slipped on a patch of slick, glassy growth. I windmilled, my heart seizing into a hard knot in my throat. Kaelen’s hand shot out, clamping on my forearm. His grip was like iron cable.

“Watch the lichen,” he said, his voice a low gravel. “It thinks it’s still water.” He didn’t wait for thanks, just released me and moved on.

That was the first rule. The city was made of memories that hadn’t figured out they were over. A patch of floor remembered being a mirror-smooth lake. A archway remembered being a solid column, and if you leaned on it, your hand might pass through. You had to read the texture, the faint, pulsing light within the crystal. You had to listen to what it thought it was.

My phone was a dead, cold slab in my pocket. No bars. No time. No Ma. Just the persistent, hollow ache where her calls should have been. It was a different kind of silence, one I’d chosen. It sat beside the other silence—the one that watched us.

The whispers were constant now. That dry, rustling chorus, the sound of pages turning in a library that had burned. They came from the deep shadows between the spires, from the jagged mouths of empty windows. Not words. Just the shape where words had been. They tugged at the edges of my thoughts, trying to unspool them. *Remember this? Remember the taste of that? Remember the day you broke?* They were hungry for the noise of me.

“Ignore them,” Elara murmured, her shoulder close to mine as we navigated a narrow ledge that spiraled inward. “They’re echoes of echoes. They can’t take anything you don’t give.”

“What do they want?” I asked, my voice too loud in the hushed, sighing air.

“To be remembered. Truly remembered. It’s the only thing that fills the hollow. But they’ve forgotten what they are, so they just… consume the feeling of being known.”

We reached a relative flat—a wide, tilted plaza of milky crystal that looked out over the cavern’s dizzying interior. Other spires hung like frozen lightning strikes in the gloom. Bridges of faint light, weaker and sicker than the ones above, spanned some of the gaps. This was the belly. This was where the world had gone to forget itself.

Kaelen stopped, raising a fist. We froze. He was staring at a cluster of darker shapes nestled in the lee of a fractured dome. They weren’t crystal. They were scrap metal, ceramic plating, woven wire—a nest. A human nest.

“Scavengers,” Kaelen said, the word tasting sour. “Wait here.”

“They’re your people,” Elara said, not a question.

“They’re survivors. There’s a difference.”

He approached slowly, hands open and away from his body. A figure uncurled from the nest. Then another. They were thin, wrapped in layers of patched fabric and scavenged polymer. Their eyes were huge in gaunt faces, reflecting the weak light. They watched Kaelen with a flat, calculating stillness that made my skin prickle. This wasn’t Ms. Clara arguing with a delivery bot. This was the argument after the bot wins.

A short, sharp conversation happened in that hissing, sighing language. I caught a few words Kaelen had used earlier—*surface*, *key*, *Silence*. One of the scavengers, a woman with a shock of white hair and a face like a knotted rope, looked past Kaelen directly at me. Her gaze was a physical weight. It wasn’t curiosity. It was an appraisal. She was weighing my boots, my jacket, the fear on my face. Calculating my worth in calories and conflict.

She barked a laugh, said something that made the others tense. Kaelen’s back went rigid. He turned and walked back to us, his expression carved from stone.

“We move. Now.”

“What did she say?” Elara asked.

“She said the surface-rat looks soft. And that The Silence pays well for soft things that carry hard secrets.”

We didn’t run. Running on memory-architecture was a good way to step into a patch that remembered being a bottomless pit. But we moved with a new, urgent rhythm. Kaelen led us off the main plaza, into a canyon formed by two leaning towers. The whispers here were different—sharper, more focused. They coiled around my ankles like cold mist.

The canyon narrowed. Our path became a series of crumbling steps that flowed up a wall. We were climbing again, the world tilting ninety degrees. My lungs burned. The journal in my backpack felt like it was gaining weight with every step.

That’s when the humming started.

It wasn’t part of the whisper-chorus. This was a clean, mechanical sound, a vibration that came up through the crystal and into the bones of my teeth. Kaelen froze, pressing himself against the wall.

“Drones,” he breathed.

They slid into the far end of the canyon, three of them. They were shaped like manta rays, sleek and matte black, absorbing the little light there was. No eyes. Just a smooth, sensor-studded underbelly. They moved in absolute silence except for that low, invasive hum. The Silence’s vacuum cleaners, coming to suck up the noise.

They floated down the canyon, slow and methodical. One paused near a crevice. A thin, blue laser scanned the area. A whisper from the crevice rose in pitch, became a warble of static, and was abruptly cut off. The drone hummed, satisfied, and moved on. It had deleted an echo.

They were heading right for us.

“Can’t go back,” Kaelen muttered, eyes darting. “The scavengers will be waiting. Can’t go forward. They’ll scan us. The key… they’ll smell it on you.”

“What do we do?” Elara’s hand went to the tool at her belt.

“We don’t fight. You can’t break them. They just re-knit.” He looked at me, a desperate, wild look. “You have the key. You have a memory. A strong one. You have to… *show* it to them.”

“What?”

“The key isn’t a physical thing! It’s a… a frequency. A pattern. Project a memory. A loud one. It might scramble their senses, make us invisible. But it has to be *loud*.”

The drones were fifty yards away. The leading one’s sensor array pulsed softly, sweeping the canyon floor.

My mind went blank. A loud memory. Ma’s voice, yelling. The blare of Leon’s speakers. The screech of the subway. But those were sounds, not the memory itself. The memory was the feeling. The trapped feeling. The before-the-change feeling.

The hook in my chest twisted.

I fumbled the key from my pocket. It was warm, almost hot. The drones’ hum deepened. They’d detected it. They accelerated, smooth and deadly.

I didn’t know what I was doing. I just clutched the key and closed my eyes. I didn’t think of the memory. I *fell* into it. The feeling of the chain-link fence against my forehead. The taste of metal and hopelessness in my mouth. The crushing weight of a future that was already written. The scream that built in my chest with no way out. The moment before I decided to become a splinter.

I didn’t scream. I pushed the *shape* of that scream out through the key.

The world didn’t go silent. It *rippled*.

A visible wave, like heat haze, pulsed out from me. It passed over the crystal walls, and for a second, they flickered, showing not broken spires but the grim, gray face of the warehouse district under a bruised sky. The chain-link fence superimposed itself over the canyon. The scent of wet asphalt and garbage flooded the air, overwhelming the ozone.

The drones hit the ripple.

They didn’t explode. They *stuttered*. Their smooth hum fractured into a jagged, glitching screech. Their flight paths went erratic, spinning them into the walls, into each other. Their sensor-lights blazed chaotic patterns. One drone’s nose crumpled against the crystal. It righted itself, humming in a broken, looping whine, and began scanning the wall intently, over and over, as if the memory of a fence was the only true thing in the world.

The memory-flicker died. The canyon was just a canyon again. The two functional drones were still twitching, disoriented. The third was married to the wall.

“Now,” Kaelen hissed, and we were moving, scrambling up the last of the steps and out of the canyon, leaving the dysfunctional hum behind.

We didn’t stop until we’d crossed two more swaying, light-bridges and dropped into a deep, shadowy crevice between three massive foundation stones. We collapsed there, breathing hard. My hands were shaking violently. The key in my palm felt scorched.

Elara was staring at me. “You… you painted over reality.”

“It was defensive,” Kaelen said, but he was looking at me differently too. The cynicism was still there, but underneath it was a spark of raw, desperate hope. “A parlor trick. But it worked. They’ll reboot and report, but it bought us time.” He leaned his head back against the stone. “We can’t stay in the open. We need to get to the Nest.”

“The Nest?” I asked, my voice ragged.

“Where the people who haven’t given up yet try to remember how to be people.” He pushed himself up. “It’s not far. And the woman from the scavengers, Rell, she won’t follow there. Even she respects the borders.”

The Nest wasn’t a place you found. It was a place you were allowed to see. Kaelen led us through a labyrinth of collapsed infrastructure, through a runoff pipe that remembered being a grand artery, and finally to a seemingly solid wall of dark, smoky quartz. He placed his palm on it and hummed a few low notes. The memory of the wall shifted, accepting the new data. It remembered being a doorway.

It irised open.

The smell hit me first. Not ozone. Not dust. The smell of bodies, of cooking, of mildew and machine oil and something herbal. The smell of life, crammed into a hiding place. Sound followed—a low murmur of voices, the clang of metal, a child’s distant cry. It was the messiest, most beautiful noise I’d heard since my own block.

We stepped into a cavern within the cavern. It was the hollow base of one of the largest spires, its walls curving up into darkness. Structures were built here—not from memory-crystal, but from real, scavenged things. Sheets of metal formed lean-tos. Wires hung with faded cloth created private spaces. Small, stubborn gardens of luminous fungi grew in cracked basins. Fires burned in contained ceramic pits, their smoke drawn up through fissures in the ceiling.

People moved through the space. Maybe a hundred of them. They were thin, worn, but their movements had purpose. Mending, cooking, arguing, tending. Their eyes weren’t the hollow pits of the scavengers. They were tired, but they were lit.

Heads turned as we entered. The murmur died. Every eye fixed on us, and most specifically, on me—the stranger in the wrong clothes.

A man emerged from the crowd. He was older, his back bent, his face a map of deep lines. He wore a patched coat that might have once been military. His eyes, a pale, watery blue, swept over Kaelen and Elara before landing on me.

“Kaelen. You bring guests.” His voice was like stone grinding on stone.

“Caretaker,” Kaelen said, with a deference he’d shown no one else. “This is Alex. From the surface. He carries the key.”

A ripple went through the crowd. The word *key* passed from mouth to mouth in a sigh.

The Caretaker stepped closer. He didn’t ask for the key. He just looked at me, his gaze peeling back layers. “Why?”

The question was too big. Why was I here? Why did I have it? Why any of it?

“Because someone has to remember,” I said, the words coming out hoarse. “Or else it all just… echoes.”

He held my gaze for a long moment. Then he gave a slow, almost imperceptible nod. “The Vault’s light dims. The Silence grows bolder. They’ve taken the western spire. They are… *quieting* it. Room by room.” He turned, gesturing for us to follow. “Come. You will see. Then you will understand what you carry.”

He led us through the Nest, to its far edge, where the living space gave way to a sheer drop. A wide, arched window, long ago shattered, looked out across the cavern.

He pointed.

In the distance, one of the great spires, its light once a steady silver, now pulsed with a sickly, intermittent gray. Around it, like black mites, swarmed dozens of the manta-ray drones. As we watched, a pulse of deep violet light erupted from the base of the spire. It swept upward. Where it passed, the spire’s inherent glow didn’t just dim. It *vanished*. A whole section of the structure went dark, not just empty, but void. The whispers from that section didn’t just fade. They were erased, replaced by a perfect, absolute silence that felt louder than any scream.

“They are not just hunting echoes,” the Caretaker said, his voice hollow. “They are carving out the past itself. Making a world where nothing ever was. Where no one can ever be from.” He looked at me, his ancient eyes full of a terrible clarity. “Your key is not just a tool for doors. It is a seed. The last seed of a forest they burned. You must get to the Vault before they finish their work. Because when the last light goes out…”

He didn’t finish. He didn’t need to.

I looked at my hands again. A kid’s hands. Stained with tunnel grime, now tinged with the weird energy of the key. They held a dead man’s book. They held the memory of a scream.

From across the Nest, I heard a familiar sound. Not a whisper. A real, wet, coughing cry. A baby. Someone shushed it, rocked it. The sound was frantic, alive, fighting against the immense, swallowing quiet of the dead spire in the distance.

The hook in my chest wasn’t pulling anymore. It had anchored. Right here, in this desperate, messy, real place. I was the splinter. And my job was to dig in.

But as I stared at the dying spire, at the swarm of silent mites consuming history itself, a cold realization seeped into my bones.

They weren’t just going to try to stop me.

They were going to try to make it so I had never come here at all.



---

# Chapter 7

## **CHAPTER 7**

The Nest breathed in shallow, panicked hitches. The distant spire’s dead patch was a hole in the world’s lung, a sucking silence that made every other sound—the baby’s cry, the rustle of cloth, the low, worried hum of the gathered adults—feel thin and temporary. The Caretaker’s words hung in the damp air, not fading, just settling into the grime on our skin. *A world where nothing ever was.*

I looked at my hands. Kid’s hands. They didn’t feel like a seed. They felt like a stain.

A woman pushed through the small crowd that had gathered in the chamber of shattered glass. She moved like water finding a crack—smooth, inevitable, leaving no space for argument. Her hair was a practical, dark braid coiled tight against her skull, and her eyes were the color of old flint. She wore patched gear, but it was clean, the lines sharp. This was Elara. I knew it before the Caretaker inclined his head to her.

“You saw,” she said. It wasn’t a question. Her voice was low, worn smooth by use, like a stone in a river.

“We saw,” the Caretaker confirmed. “The Hollowing has begun in earnest. The Spire of Lament is… unmade.”

Elara’s gaze landed on me. It wasn’t hostile. It was an assessment, like she was weighing a tool she’d found in the dirt. “And this is the splinter.”

“He carries the key.”

“I see that.” She stepped closer. The air around her smelled of ozone and dried herbs. “The mark is on him. Faint, but it’s there. Like a fingerprint on a dusty window.” She didn’t touch me, just studied my face. “You understand nothing, do you?”

“I understand I’m in over my head,” I said, the words scraping out. “I understand that thing in the distance is eating ghosts. I understand my phone is probably blowing up back in a world that sweats rain two hours after it falls. That’s what I understand.”

A flicker at the corner of her mouth. Not a smile. An acknowledgment. “Good. Understanding everything is the first step to being useless.” She turned to the others. “We can’t watch from the windows anymore. The Hollowing isn’t a siege. It’s an erasure. They take a spire, and the memories it held… they don’t fade. They are *retconned*. The people who lived them start to forget. They become thinner. Less real. Until they blink out, and no one remembers they were ever there to forget.”

A man with a mechanic’s gloves tucked into his belt shook his head. “So we fight for our ghosts?”

“We fight for our *ground*,” Elara corrected, her voice cutting. “The past is the soil. You take away the soil, nothing grows. Not a person, not a thought, not a future. The city—both cities—are built on layers of what was. They are scraping it down to bare rock.” She pointed a finger at the dead spire, visible through the great broken window. “That is the future. A perfect, silent, empty page.”

The Caretaker stirred. “The counter-measure exists. It has always existed.”

“The Heartspring,” Elara said, and the word left a reverent quiet in its wake.

I saw it in their faces then. Not hope, exactly. Something harder. More desperate. The look of people who’ve run out of corners to back into, who see only one door left, and it’s rusted shut.

“The what?” I asked.

Elara’s flinty eyes came back to me. “The engine. The first memory. The moment the old world broke and this one… *sprang*. It’s a well of potential, buried deep in the city’s core. It’s what gives the spires their light. What lets the echoes whisper. It’s failing. Has been for a long time. Drying up. The Hollowing… it’s not the cause. It’s a symptom. They’re accelerating the process, sucking the last moisture from the stone.”

The Caretaker nodded, his many-lensed eyes dim. “To reignite it is to replenish the source. To give the city a heartbeat again. It would push the Hollowing back. Heal the spires. Stabilize the boundary between your world and this one.”

“Sounds like a plan,” I said. “So go reignite it.”

The silence that followed was thick enough to taste. It was the mechanic who spoke, pulling a greasy cloth from his pocket. “It’s not a switch. It’s a ritual. A communion. Requires a specific… resonance. A bloodline. The old guardians who first tended the Spring.”

All of them were looking at me again. The hook in my chest twisted.

“No,” I said.

“The key chose you,” the Caretaker said softly. “It is not a random artifact. It is a lodestone. It finds the lineage. However diluted. However forgotten.”

I thought of my mother’s face, tight with worry over gas bills and grocery coupons. I thought of my father, a faded photograph and a name on a missing persons report. A quiet man, Ma said. A man who liked old maps and long walks. A man who sometimes stared at nothing for hours. I thought of the itch in my own bones, the one that made me walk for miles, tracing alleys and sewer grates, feeling for a seam in the world.

“My father,” I said, the words ash in my mouth.
