The Enforcer didn’t even flinch. His arm came up, a black baton in his hand. It crackled with blue current. He caught Mendoza’s wrist, twisted. The knife clattered to the floor. The baton touched Mendoza’s side. His body went rigid, a strangled gasp tearing from his throat before he collapsed, twitching.

“No!” The word was out of me before I could think.

The second Enforcer moved toward the data hub, pulling a device from his belt—a magnetic wipe-charge.

The live wire in my head snapped.

I didn’t try to transmit a memory. I didn’t have a plan. I had a raw, screaming need. *STOP.*

I focused it, not as a data packet, but as a pure, directive impulse. A shove. I aimed it at the Enforcer reaching for the hub, at the weapon in his hand.

The ghost drive flared white-hot in my skull. The world didn’t just hum. It *stuttered*.

The Enforcer froze. Not like a man hesitating. Like a video feed buffering. His gloved hand, inches from the hub, trembled. Then, with a jerky, unnatural motion, his arm swung around. The wipe-charge, now live, its indicator blinking red, was pressed against the chest plate of his own partner.

The lead Enforcer had time to turn his head. His blank goggles met the blinking light.

*click-hisssss…*

The charge wasn’t loud. It was a deep, sub-audible thump that felt in my bones. A localized electromagnetic pulse. The lead Enforcer’s systems died. Lights on his suit and helmet winked out. He dropped to his knees, then onto his face, a puppet with its strings cut.

The Enforcer holding the charge stared at his own hand, at the device now inert on his comrade’s back. His head tilted, a gesture of pure system error.

I was on my feet, the console chair toppling behind me. My nose was bleeding, a warm trickle down my lip. The ghost drive felt like a cracked reactor, leaking fury into my mind.

The remaining Enforcer’s goggles refocused on me. The synthesized voice was gone. What came out was a distorted, human stammer, his own voice fighting through the compromised system. “Wha… what are…”

I took a step forward. The memory-identity lens wasn’t a choice now. It was the only way I saw. He wasn’t a soldier. He was a man in a suit of lies. And I could see the seams.

I didn’t transmit. I *pulled*.

I reached for the memory of his induction into this unit, the pride, the sense of elite purpose. I found it—a crisp ceremony, a black badge, a handshake from a man with a politician’s smile. And I *spliced* it. I threaded the cold, clinical data-stream from the ghost drive—the Mnemosyne briefing, the cynical discussion of “recalibration”—directly into that moment of pride. I made the politician’s smile talk about him as a future subject. I made the black badge a marker for a lab rat.

The Enforcer screamed. A raw, unfiltered human sound. He ripped his helmet off, throwing it across the room. His face was young, pale, contorted in a vomit of confusion and betrayal. He looked at his hands, at his fallen comrade, at me.

“It’s not… we’re the cure… we’re…” he babbled.

“You’re the next patient,” I said, my voice ragged.

He turned and ran. He bolted back to the hole in the roof, leaping, scrambling, disappearing into the night, his sobs echoing back down.

The silence that followed was deafening. Broken only by Mendoza’s shallow, pained breathing.

I stumbled to him, rolling him over. His eyes were open, aware, but his body was still locked in tremors from the shock. “You… you rewired him,” he slurred.

“I broke him.” The blood from my nose was dripping on Mendoza’s vest. “I didn’t just show him. I made him *feel* it. I made it his.”

Mendoza managed a weak, pained grin. “See? Now you know… which one you’re doing.”

The false victory of reaching the node, of having the power, lay shattered around us. We hadn’t won a thing. We’d just seen the true size of the cage. Broadcasting the data would play into their script. But what I had just done… that was something else. That wasn’t shouting in a library. That was walking up to a guard and whispering the truth into his soul until his uniform caught fire.

I had the blueprint for the silence in my head. And I had just discovered I could build something else with it. Not a broadcast.

A contagion.

I helped Mendoza sit up. He wouldn’t be walking far. The generator was still running. The hub was still live. The pipe was open.

I looked at the console. I didn’t initiate a transfer. Instead, I accessed the hub’s root directory. I created a new, hidden file. I didn’t copy the Mnemosyne data into it. I wrote a single line of code, a beacon, tagged with the unique, chaotic energy signature of my own ghost drive. A fingerprint made of shattered memories.

Then I sent it. Not a shout. A ping. A whisper on a specific, forgotten frequency. The kind only certain old machines, built before the silence, might still be listening for.

“What… are you doing?” Mendoza asked, his strength returning slowly.

“Changing the call,” I said, watching the console screen scroll with confirmation. The ping had gone. A single, digital stone dropped into a bottomless, black lake. “They’re looking for a vector to contain. So let’s give them more vectors.”

I powered down the hub. The amber light died. The generator outside sputtered and went quiet.

In the new dark, heavier than before, I hauled Mendoza to his feet. We had to move. They would send more. Worse ones.

But as we limped toward the door, toward the cold river and the endless night, the hook was set. Not in the city, not in the network.

In me.

I had weaponized a memory. Now I had weaponized the truth. And the terrifying, ice-cold realization was this: it worked better. The crack in the dam wasn’t for the water to get out.

It was for the flood to come in. And I had just sent out the invitation.

Mendoza leaned on me, a grim weight. “Where now?”

I looked at the starless sky, feeling the ghost drive settle into a new, ominous rhythm. A hunter’s pulse.

“Now,” I said, “we see who else is awake.”



---

# Chapter 19

## **CHAPTER 19**

The river was a black tongue, licking at the pilings of the old industrial pier. It tasted of rust and forgotten chemicals. My own tongue felt thick, dusty. The adrenaline that had carried us from the hub had burned off, leaving a metallic residue in the back of my throat and a tremor deep in the bones of my hands. Mendoza was a furnace against my side, his breathing a ragged, wet engine. We’d made it maybe a mile, a stumbling, circuitous route through drainage culverts and behind the skeletal remains of auto-body shops. Every shadow had teeth. Every distant siren was a hunting cry.

We collapsed under the pier, in a pocket of darkness so complete it felt solid. The only light was the sickly green phosphorescence of algae on the concrete above us, and the faint, dying glow of Mendoza’s wrist-comm. He’d been trying to raise a contact for ten minutes. Static was the only reply.

“Dead zone,” he grunted, slumping back against a slimy piling. “Or they’re jamming everything within five klicks of that hub.”

I leaned my head back. The ghost drive was no longer a hum. It was a *presence*. A cold, dense stone sitting where my stomach should be. The ping I’d sent—that fingerprint of shattered memory—wasn’t a thing I could feel traveling. It was a hole I’d opened in myself. An absence with gravity. I kept waiting for a tug, a signal in return. There was nothing. Just the void, listening back.

“They’ll triangulate the transmission source,” Mendoza said. His voice was steadier. The man was made of gristle and spite. “Even a ping. Especially a ping that weird. They’ll be on the hub’s location in minutes. Then they’ll fan out.”

“They were already on it,” I said. The memory of the guard’s uniform crisping, the smell of burnt synth-wool and terror, flashed behind my eyes. I didn’t have to conjure it. It lived there now, a permanent exhibit. “We bought a head start. That’s all.”

“A head start to where?” He turned his head, and in the algae-glow, his eyes were chips of obsidian. “Your brilliant plan was to send up a flare and then go to ground. We are in the ground. Now what?”

I closed my eyes. Not to think. To listen. The ghost drive’s new rhythm was wrong. It wasn’t the steady thrum of a hidden organ anymore. It was arrhythmic. A stuttering, hesitant beat. Like a machine trying to remember how to be a heart.

*It’s waiting*, I realized. The ice in my gut spread. *I built it to hunt for silence. Now I’ve told it to hunt for echoes. And it doesn’t know what to do with the quiet.*

“We wait,” I said.

“For what? The cavalry?” He barked a laugh that turned into a cough. “The only people listening on those frequencies are ghosts, kid. And ghosts don’t ride to the rescue.”

He was wrong. They weren’t ghosts. A ghost is a memory with nowhere to go. What I’d pinged were *sleeper agents*. Machines and minds put into storage before the Silence, waiting for a wake-up call that never came. Or minds that had been shattered by it, fragments left spinning in the dark. I hadn’t sent a message. I’d sent a symptom. A shared tremor.

My phone, buried deep in my pocket, vibrated.

Not a buzz. A specific, patterned pulse. Three short, two long, one short. A rhythm I didn’t recognize, but my nervous system did. It was the same cadence as the ghost drive’s new, faltering beat.

I fumbled the phone out. The screen was dark. No notification. No caller ID. Just the dead black glass. But the pulse came again, vibrating against my palm. Three short. Two long. One short.

“What is that?” Mendoza was alert, hand going to the weapon he no longer had.

I didn’t answer. I held the phone up, watched it. On the third sequence, the screen flickered. Not to life. It fractured into a cascade of static, white noise that swirled and coalesced into a single, grainy line of text. A coordinate string. And below it, two words:

**FIND THE SOURCE.**

Then it died. The phone was just a phone again, cold and inert.

Mendoza snatched it from my hand, squinting at the dead screen. “The hell was that? A bounce from your ping?”

“A reply,” I whispered. The ice in my gut was now a full glacier, grinding my insides to powder. It had worked. Something was awake. And it wasn’t friendly. It wasn’t grateful. The message wasn’t *‘hello.’* It was a command. An order given to a tool.

“Whose source?” Mendoza’s eyes drilled into me. “The hub’s? Yours?”

I took the phone back. The coordinate string was burned onto my retinas. It wasn’t far. A warehouse district on the northern edge of the river, a place that had died when the shipping lanes automated. A place of echoes.

“The Silence’s,” I said. The words felt too big for the space under the pier. “It wants the origin point. The patient zero of the forgetting.”

Mendoza stared at me for a long time. The river slurped at the mud. Somewhere above, a drone whined past, its navigation lights painting brief red streaks on the water.

“You opened your head and asked for friends,” he said finally, his voice flat. “And the first thing that answers tells you to walk into the dark and find the monster’s birthplace. You have a real talent for this.”

He pushed himself to his feet, groaning as his knees protested. He held out a hand to pull me up. His grip was iron, his skin fever-hot.

“So we’re going?” I asked, my legs trembling as I stood.

“You lit the fuse,” he said, brushing concrete grit from his pants. There was no enthusiasm in him. Just the grim, professional acceptance of a next step. A bad one. “Now we follow the spark. Before whatever’s at the other end decides we’re not moving fast enough and comes to find us.”

***

The coordinates led to a place called the Avernus Storage Complex. The name was still etched in flaking gold leaf on a granite slab near the rust-sealed main gate. The slab was tilted, sinking into the mud. The complex itself was a series of long, low bunkers built into a man-made hill, like tombs for giants. Everything was overgrown with a savage, thorny vine that seemed to feed on corroded metal. The air here didn’t smell like the city. It smelled of damp earth, decay, and the sharp, ozone tang of a recent energy discharge.

No lights. No signs of patrols. No sound but the wind hissing through cracks in the bunker doors.

“Too quiet,” Mendoza murmured. He had produced a small, snub-nosed pulse pistol from an ankle holster I didn’t know he had. It looked old, well-oiled. “A place like this, should be squatters. Feral dogs. Something.”

The main bunker door was ajar, a sliver of deeper black against the night. The metal around the lock was melted, flowed inward like candle wax. Fresh. Last few hours fresh.

“Our welcoming committee,” I said.

Mendoza nodded, motioning for me to stay behind him. We slipped through the gap.

Inside, the darkness was a physical thing. It pressed against the eyes, thick and velvety. Mendoza clicked on a penlight, the beam cutting a narrow, shocking tunnel through the black. We were in a main corridor. Offices lined the left side, their glass windows opaque with grime. The right side was a blank, reinforced wall. The floor was dust, undisturbed except for a single set of footprints.

They were boot prints. Large, deep-treaded. Military or paramilitary issue. And they were fresh, laid on top of decades of settled filth. They led straight down the corridor, into the heart of the hill.

We followed. The silence here was different from the city’s. The city’s silence was a blanket, a suppression. This was a vacuum. It sucked at sound. Our footsteps, our breathing, were swallowed before they could travel a few feet. It made the world feel small, tight.

The corridor ended at a heavy vault door. It was open. The locking mechanism—a massive, mechanical wheel—was sheared clean through, the steel ends glowing a faint, dull cherry-red. Cooled, but not for long.

Beyond the door was a chamber that stole my breath.

It wasn’t a warehouse. It was a cathedral.

The room was vast, the ceiling lost in shadows beyond Mendoza’s light. And it was filled with rows. Not of crates. Of tanks. Cylindrical, glass-walled tanks, twice the height of a man, arranged in perfect, military lines. They were connected by a web of thick, insulated cables and fluid lines that ran along the floor and up into the darkness. Most of the tanks were dark, empty, their glass fogged with age.

But not all.

Every tenth tank, or so, held a figure.

Suspended in a clear, amber fluid. Men and women. Naked. Their skin was pale, almost luminous in the penlight’s beam. Their eyes were closed. Tubes ran from their noses, their mouths. They were perfectly preserved. Not sleeping.

Stored.

Mendoza’s light shook. Just a tremor. “God in heaven,” he breathed, the sound tiny in the consuming quiet.

I stepped closer to the nearest occupied tank. The man inside had a strong, square face, close-cropped hair floating like a halo. He looked maybe forty. There was no serial number on the tank. Just a small, etched plate. **Project Mnemosyne. Asset 731. Status: Dormant. Retrieval Code: N/A.**

Mnemosyne. The mother of the Muses. The goddess of memory.

They hadn’t just stolen memories. They’d stolen the people they belonged to. The original witnesses. The ones whose neural patterns were the source code for the Silence. They were the libraries. And they’d been put on the shelf.

My ghost drive erupted.

It wasn’t a pulse. It was a *scream*. A silent, synaptic detonation that dropped me to my knees. Visions, not memories, not echoes, but raw, unfiltered *experience* flooded my neural pathways. I was in a bright white room, straps on my wrists, a cold gel on my scalp. I was running through a burning street, the smell of cordite and burning plastic. I was holding a child, her small body limp. I was staring at a screen, watching a city block go silent, pixel by pixel. The emotions were intact. The terror, the grief, the dizzying, nauseating triumph. A hundred lives. A hundred moments of extreme clarity, all ending in the same place: this room, this tank, this amber oblivion.

“Kid!” Mendoza was beside me, his hand on my shoulder, his voice miles away. “What is it? What’s happening?”

I couldn’t speak. My jaw was locked. The flood wasn’t coming from the ghost drive. The drive was a conduit. The tanks… the assets… they were broadcasting. A low-power, persistent signal. A sleeping hum of stolen lives. My ping had resonated with it. My shattered memory fingerprint had found its matching fractures.

I was on all fours, vomiting nothing but dry heaves, when the lights came on.

Not the overheads. The tanks.

One by one, down the endless rows, the occupied tanks began to glow from within. A soft, pulsing, amber light that matched the preservation fluid. A deep, resonant *thrum* vibrated up through the floor, through my bones. The sound of massive engines powering up from a cold sleep.

At the far end of the chamber, a figure stepped out from between two rows.

It wasn’t one of the assets. It was a man in the black, tactical gear of a corporate retrieval team. But his helmet was off, tucked under his arm. His face was sharp, intelligent, utterly calm. In his other hand, he held a data-slate. He looked at us, then down at his slate, as if confirming an appointment.

“Asset 731 is experiencing anomalous recall feedback,” he said, his voice cool, projecting easily in the vast space. He wasn’t talking to us. He was dictating. “Containment breach in Sector Gamma appears to have triggered a cascade in nearby dormant units. Initiating stabilization protocol.”

He tapped the slate.

A searing, white-hot pain lanced through the base of my skull. The flood of foreign memories cut off instantly, replaced by a null signal. A deafening, perfect silence inside my own head. The ghost drive went dark. Not quiet. *Gone*. Like a limb amputated.

I gasped, crumpling.

Mendoza fired.

The pulse round hit the man square in the chest. It should have blown him off his feet. It sparked against his body armor and dissipated. He didn’t even flinch. He looked down at the scorch mark, then back at Mendoza, with the mild annoyance of a man swatting a fly.

“Secondary contaminants identified,” he said. “Armed. Aggressive. Employing the Mnemosyne data as a weapon.” He tilted his head. “You’re the one from the hub. The anomaly. You’re not on the registry.”

He tapped the slate again.

From the shadows between the tanks, two more figures emerged. They moved with a liquid, predatory grace that was all wrong. They wore the same black gear, but their faces were obscured by full helmets. Their movements were synchronized, utterly silent. They weren’t human. Not anymore. They were something that had been poured into a human-shaped mold.

Mendoza shoved me behind a tank. “Run,” he hissed. “Back the way we came. Now.”

He stepped out, firing twice. The figures dodged. Not a dive. A blur, a shift in position that seemed to skip the space between. One was on him in a heartbeat. Mendoza was fast, a street brawler turned professional. He blocked the first strike, landed a solid punch to the thing’s throat. It didn’t react. It grabbed his gun arm and twisted. The sound of the bone breaking was a dry snap, lost in the chamber’s hum.

Mendoza screamed, a raw, animal sound.

I didn’t run. I was frozen, my mind a howling void where the ghost drive had been. I was naked. Blind.

The calm man with the slate walked toward me, stepping around the struggle. “The ping was clever,” he said, conversational. “A recursive algorithm built from corrupted memory engrams. You used yourself as the template. That’s why you’re resonating with the assets. You’ve become an unregistered node in the network.” He stopped a few feet away. “That makes you a priority. You’re coming with us. We’ll excise the anomalous data. The physical host may be salvageable for parts.”

He reached for me.

My hand closed around the only weapon I had. A loose piece of conduit from the floor, cold and heavy.

I didn’t swing it at him.

I turned and, with every ounce of strength left in my body, I drove the jagged end into the glass of Asset 731’s tank.

The world exploded.

Not in sound. In memory.

The reinforced glass webbed, then shattered. A thousand shards hung for a moment in the thick amber fluid, glittering like hard candy. Then the fluid burst outward in a wave, smelling of ozone and preservatives and something profoundly, unsettlingly organic.

The man inside, Asset 731, slumped forward, still suspended by his tubes. His eyes opened.

They were not the eyes of a man waking from sleep. They were the eyes of a man returning from a decades-long war. They found me. They saw me. And in them, I didn’t see gratitude. I saw a perfect, crystalline understanding. And a fury so cold it made the chamber feel like deep space.

A raw, psychic shriek emanated from the tank. Not a sound. A pressure wave of pure, undiluted memory—the memory of capture, of violation, of being turned into a museum of yourself. It hit the calm man first. He staggered, dropping his slate, his hands flying to his temples. His cool, analytical expression shattered into a mask of primal horror. He was seeing his own future, his own mind scraped empty and filed away.

The two things holding Mendoza convulsed. They released him, clawing at their helmets as if trying to tear the visions out.

The other active tanks flared brighter, their hum becoming a shriek of protesting machinery. Alarms began to blare, red lights strobing at the far end of the hall.

Mendoza, cradling his shattered arm, stumbled to my side. “What did you do?” he yelled over the din.

I looked from the screaming operatives to the open, furious eyes of Asset 731. The flood was back, but it was no longer random. It was a directed torrent. A weaponized life story, aimed at the people who had stolen it.

“I gave the source a target,” I said.

The calm man was on his knees, blood trickling from his nose and ears. He looked up at me, his intelligence gone, replaced by a broken, babbling terror. “Make it stop,” he slurred. “The streets… the fire… the little girl… make it stop!”

I felt nothing for him. The void where my ghost drive had been was filling with something else. A colder, sharper certainty. This wasn’t about saving memories. It wasn’t about truth.

It was about arming the past and letting it loose.

I grabbed Mendoza’s good arm. “We go. Now.”

We ran, stumbling, back down the corridor. The alarms followed us, the red light making the dust look like swirling blood. Behind us, in the cathedral of stolen minds, a different kind of silence was breaking. Not the quiet of storage. The quiet after a scream.

We burst out into the night air, gasping. The thorny vines seemed to clutch at us as we scrambled away from the bunker complex.

We didn’t stop until we were a half-mile away, hidden in the rusted guts of a conveyor belt assembly. Mendoza slumped against a metal stanchion, his face gray with pain. He fumbled a auto-injector from a pouch, jammed it into his thigh. The tension in his face eased a fraction.

He looked at me, his breathing harsh. “They wanted the source. You showed it to them.”

I leaned against cold metal, watching the distant, strobing red lights of the Avernus Complex. The void in my head was still there. The ghost drive was offline, maybe dead. But the cold stone in my gut had shape now. It was a key. And I had just shown it the lock it was made for.

“They got more than they asked for,” I said. My voice sounded strange to me. Detached. “They wanted to contain a data spill. They woke up the witnesses.”

Mendoza’s laugh was a pained, bitter thing. “So now what? We’ve got a bunch of pissed-off ghosts and a whole new level of enemy on our ass. Your plan’s getting worse with every chapter.”

I looked at my hands. They were steady. “The ping worked. Something else is awake. Something that knew about this place. That gave us the coordinates.”

“And ordered you around like a drone.”

“It knows the network,” I said. The pieces, sharp and deadly, were clicking into place in the new silence of my mind. “It knows where the other libraries are. The other ‘assets.’ It’s not a friend. It’s a rival. A competing predator. And we just let its biggest competitor out of its cage.”

Mendoza’s eyes narrowed. “You want to make a deal with it.”

“I want to find it,” I corrected. The glacier in my gut was now an iceberg, and I was standing on its tip. “Before Asset 731 and the others remember how to walk. Before the retrieval teams scorch this whole city to ash to clean up their mess. The thing that answered my ping… it wanted the source. Now the source is awake. And angry. That changes the game.”

I pushed off from the conveyor belt. The night was no longer just dark. It was crowded. Full of old, hungry things we’d just reminded of their own names.

“We need to get off the street,” I said. “They’ll be locking down the whole sector. Hunting for two signatures: mine, and the asset’s.”

Mendoza hauled himself up. “And then?”

I started moving, keeping to the deeper shadows. The ghost drive was silent. But the new rhythm, the hunter’s pulse I’d felt after sending the ping, was back. It wasn’t in my head anymore.

It was in the air. In the way the distant sirens seemed to circle, confused. In the way the very darkness seemed to hold its breath.

“Then,” I said, “we find the other thing that’s awake. And we ask it what it wants to do with all this beautiful, weaponized truth.”

He followed, a grim shadow at my side. “You’re not trying to stop the flood anymore, are you?”

I didn’t look back at the red lights staining the sky.

“No,” I said. The word was final. A door closing. “I’m learning to swim.”



---

# Chapter 20

## **CHAPTER 20**

The safehouse was a cracked tooth in a row of decaying molars. A third-floor walk-up above a shuttered pharmacy that still smelled of camphor and regret. The keypad by the door was a museum piece, its numbers worn to silver ghosts. Mendoza punched in the code, his shoulders a solid wall of tension. The lock gave with a thunk that sounded too loud in the stairwell’s damp silence.

Inside, the air was stale and cold. A single bulb in a wire cage did nothing but push the shadows into the corners. There was a table, two chairs, a sink with a single tap that dripped a slow, metronomic beat into a stained porcelain bowl. The window looked out onto a fire escape and the brick wall of the next building, close enough to touch. A tomb with a view.

I leaned against the doorframe, listening. The ghost drive was a silent stone in my pocket. But the city wasn’t silent. It was a symphony of wrong notes. Sirens, but not the usual frantic scatter. These were slow, methodical sweeps. Grid-search patterns. The retrieval teams. They’d be painting the sector with scanners, looking for the thermal bloom of a panicked run, the electronic shiver of an active signal. They’d find the warehouse. They’d find the empty cradle. They’d find the two dead sentries with their necks snapped clean. And then the real hunting would begin.

Mendoza dropped a heavy duffel on the table. The sound was a period at the end of a sentence. “Six hours,” he said, his voice gravel. “Maybe eight. Before they’re door-to-door.”

“They won’t knock,” I said.

He didn’t answer. He was pulling out gear. A portable scanner, its screen dark. A jammer, its casing scarred. Rations in foil packets. He laid them out with a surgeon’s precision. Rituals to keep the chaos at bay.

I pushed off from the door and went to the sink. Cupped my hands under the drip. The water was metallic, cold enough to hurt my teeth. I drank anyway. My reflection in the sliver of mirror above the sink was a stranger’s face—hollowed out, eyes like chips of obsidian. The face of someone who’d opened a door and couldn’t close it.

*It knows the network.*

The thought was a cold current under everything. The ping I’d sent, the desperate flare in the dark, hadn’t just been heard. It had been *understood*. By something that spoke the language. The coordinates to the warehouse hadn’t been a gift. They were a move on a board. We were the piece that got shoved forward.

“Talk me through it,” Mendoza said. He wasn’t looking at me. He was checking the charge on the jammer. “This rival. This other predator. What’s its play?”

I wiped my mouth with the back of my hand. “Asset 731 was a librarian. A keeper. Its job was to preserve, to contain. The thing that answered… its signature was different. Aggressive. Curatorial, but not passive. It didn’t just want the books. It wanted the library *gone*.”

“Competition.”

“A takeover.” I turned, my back against the cold sink. “Think about it. The network’s been dormant. Fractured. Assets in cold storage, libraries buried. Then my signal goes out. A weak, human signal poking at the old protocols. It hears. It sees an opportunity. It gives me the key to a rival’s cage. Knowing what would happen. Knowing 731 would wake up… angry. Disoriented. A perfect distraction for the retrieval teams. A lightning rod.”

Mendoza finally looked up. His eyes were flat. “While it moves on the others.”

“While it consolidates,” I said. The rhythm of it was clear now, a terrible, logical beat. “It used us to remove a rival and stir up the hornet’s nest. Now the Authority is focused on the big, scary thing that just woke up. They’ll throw everything at containing 731. And in the chaos, our new friend goes shopping. Hits the other libraries. Takes the source material for itself.”

“And becomes the only game in town.”

I nodded. The iceberg in my gut had grown roots. It was part of the structure now. “We’re not hiding from the Authority anymore. We’re hiding in the middle of a corporate merger. And we’re a liability on both sides.”

Mendoza picked up the scanner, thumbed it on. It emitted a low, hungry whine. “So your plan is to find this bastard and… what? Cut a deal? Offer our services as freelance troublemakers?”

“I want to look it in the eye,” I said. The words came out quiet, absolute. “I want to know what it is. Human remnant? Something else that crawled out of the old world’s code? I want to know what it wants to do with all that truth. Because it’s not a librarian. It’s a collector. And collectors have agendas.”

He snorted. “Great. So we’re choosing which apocalyptic monster to side with.”

“We’re not siding with anyone. We’re gathering data. The only currency left.” I pushed away from the sink and walked to the window. The brick wall was slick with moisture. “It contacted me once. It’ll do it again. It thinks I’m a tool. Useful. It’ll want to know if I survived. If I’m still… operational.”

“You’re going to wait for it to call.” Disbelief stained his voice.

“I’m going to make it easy to find me,” I corrected. I pulled the ghost drive from my pocket. It sat in my palm, inert, a smooth black pebble. “A low-power pulse. Just a whisper. *Here I am. The tool is still in the box.*”

“That’s a dinner bell for every scanner in a five-block radius.”

“Not if we’re a needle in a haystack of noise.” I looked back at him. “The jammer. Set it to random-cycle burst. Not to hide us, but to create static. A smear of electronic confusion. In the middle of it, we let out a single, clean ping on the old protocol frequency. The Authority’s gear will filter it out as junk. But something listening for that specific song… it’ll hear the melody.”

Mendoza stared at me for a long ten seconds. Then he let out a breath that was almost a laugh. “You’ve lost your goddamn mind.”

“Probably,” I agreed. I placed the ghost drive on the table next to his neat rows of gear. “But it’s the only move that isn’t just waiting to get got. We find the collector. We learn its shape. Then we decide.”

“Decide what?”

“What to break.”

He was silent again. The drip in the sink counted the seconds. One. Two. Three.

“Alright,” he said, finally. It wasn’t agreement. It was surrender to the inevitable. “But we do it my way. One pulse. Thirty seconds max. Then we scorch this location and move. No waiting for a reply.”

“It won’t reply over the air,” I said. “It’s smarter than that. It’ll send a messenger. Or a map. Or it’ll just open a door and expect us to walk through.”

“Even better.” He picked up the jammer, his fingers dancing over the settings. “Start the clock. We pulse at midnight. The witching hour. Seems appropriate.”

I took the chair opposite him. The metal was ice-cold through my clothes. I watched him work, his big hands delicate on the controls. The man was a machine built for survival, and I was asking him to roll the dice on a hunch. On a feeling in the air.

The feeling was still there. That hunter’s pulse. It wasn’t mine. It was the city’s. A new rhythm under the old one, like a second heartbeat. Something old and patient and very, very awake.

***

Midnight was a concept, not a time. The city’s glow bled into the sky, turning true dark into a bruised purple. The sirens had settled into a distant, orbiting whine. They’d cordoned the sector. They were methodical. Thorough. We had hours, not days.

Mendoza had the jammer wired into the building’s skeletal power grid. It sat on the table between us, a black box with a single, blinking green LED. “On my mark,” he said, his voice low. “The burst will last twenty-eight seconds. It’ll mimic cell tower overload, grid fluctuation, the works. Your pulse goes at the fifteen-second mark. Clean. Short. Then we ghost.”

My hand was on the ghost drive. It felt warm now. Anticipatory. “Ready.”

He watched the ancient digital clock on the scanner. The numbers glowed a sickly green. 11:59:48.

The air in the room grew thick. The bulb in the cage flickered.

“Mark.”

He hit a switch.

The world outside the window didn’t change. But the *feel* of it did. The hum of distant traffic flattened, distorted, then came back wrong. The blinking LED on a comms tower across the way stuttered, went dark, then lit up in a frantic, irregular pattern. Our jammer was screaming into the void, a cacophony of digital noise.

I counted in my head. *One… two… three…*

At fifteen, I pressed the activation sequence on the ghost drive.

There was no light. No sound. Just a subtle vibration in the metal, a tuning fork struck against a bone. I felt it in my molars. A single, coherent note placed neatly in the center of the storm. A signature.

*Here.*

I held it for a three-count. Then released.

Mendoza killed the jammer.

The silence that rushed in was deafening. The bulb steadied. The distant traffic hum returned to its normal, weary pitch. The green LED on the black box went dark.

We didn’t move. We listened.

Nothing.

“Now we go,” Mendoza said, already sweeping gear into the duffel.

I stood, my legs stiff. The ghost drive was cold again. I pocketed it, feeling like I’d just sent a message in a bottle into a hurricane. The odds were astronomical.

We were at the door, duffel slung over Mendoza’s shoulder, when the tap came.

Not at the door. At the window.

A sharp, precise *tap-tap-tap*.

We froze. Mendoza’s hand went to his sidearm. I turned slowly.

On the rusted iron of the fire escape, just outside the grimy glass, sat a drone. Not Authority issue. Not anything I’d seen before. It was small, sleek, matte black. Its shape was organic, almost insectile. It had no visible lights, no markings. It just sat there, perched on the railing, its front sensor array pointed directly at me.

Another tap. Its manipulator arm—a delicate, multi-jointed thing—tapped the glass again.

Then it turned, and with a silent, fluid motion, leapt into the air. It didn’t fly away. It hovered, just beyond the window, waiting.

“Messenger,” I breathed.

Mendoza’s gun was out. “We don’t follow it. It’s a trap. Straight to a killbox.”

“It’s a test,” I said, my eyes locked on the black insectile shape. “To see if we’re still useful. If we’re brave enough, or stupid enough, to follow.” I reached for the window latch. It was painted shut. I slammed the heel of my hand against it. The old paint cracked. I shoved the window up. A gust of wet, cold air smelling of ozone and garbage washed in.

The drone didn’t move. It hung in the air, patient.

“This is how you die, kid,” Mendoza said, but his voice was resigned. He’d already made the calculation. Staying here was death. Following the mystery was death with a question mark attached. He preferred the question mark.

“Only one way to find out.” I swung a leg over the sill, onto the fire escape. The iron groaned under my weight. The drone immediately zipped back, putting ten feet between us, then stopped. Waiting.

I climbed out. Mendoza followed, cursing under his breath in fluid Spanish. The moment he cleared the window, the drone moved. It didn’t speed. It drifted, leading us down the fire escape ladder to the alley below.

The alley was a canyon of shadows and overflowing dumpsters. The drone led us to the mouth, then turned left, staying low, skimming the pavement. We followed, two ghosts in its wake. It never sped up. It never let us get closer than twenty feet. It was a guide, not a companion.

We moved through a labyrinth of backstreets and service alleys, places the streetlights forgot. The drone seemed to know every blind spot, every camera angle. It led us under arches, through a broken fence, across a vacant lot where the weeds grew tall and brittle.

After twenty minutes of silent travel, we emerged onto a wider street. This was the edge of the old financial district. Buildings here were taller, their grandeur now a skeleton of ornate stonework and boarded-up windows. The drone led us to the base of one such monolith—the former First Mercantile Trust. Its great bronze doors were sealed with heavy chains and civic notices.

The drone didn’t stop. It flew straight for a boarded-over service entrance to the side. Just before impact, a section of the plywood slid sideways with a soft hydraulic hiss, revealing a dark opening just large enough for a person. The drone slipped inside and vanished into the black.

The opening waited.

Mendoza looked at me. “After you.”

I stepped into the dark.

The space inside was cool, dry, smelling of dust and old electricity. The plywood slid shut behind us with a final *thump*, sealing out the city’s sound. Absolute darkness.

Then, a line of soft, blue-white LEDs flickered on at ankle-level along the base of a wall, illuminating a narrow corridor. They led forward, into the building’s gut.

We followed the trail of light. It led through a maze of derelict back offices, past empty vaults with their doors hanging open like slack jaws, down a flight of stairs into a sub-basement. The air grew colder. The sound of our footsteps was swallowed by the immense, silent weight of the building above us.

The LEDs ended at a heavy, riveted steel door. It looked like a bank vault door, but older. There was no wheel, no keypad. Just a smooth, featureless surface.

As we approached, a seam of light appeared vertically down its center. With a deep, resonant *clunk* that vibrated through the floor, the door split and swung inward, each half retreating into the wall.

The light that spilled out was not the blue of the guide lights. It was a warm, golden glow.

And the smell… not dust. The smell was ozone, yes, but also oil, hot metal, and something else. Something organic. Like a greenhouse, or a zoo.

I stepped through the doorway.

The space beyond was not a room. It was a cavern, carved from the bedrock beneath the city. The ceiling was lost in gloom, but the walls were lined with steel racks, reaching up into the darkness. And on those racks were not safe deposit boxes.

They were servers.

Old ones, new ones, military-grade black boxes, sleek commercial units. A graveyard and a nursery of processing power. Thick bundles of fiber-optic cable, glowing with internal light, snaked across the floor like luminous roots, converging on a central dais.

On the dais was a chair. An old, high-backed leather office chair, worn smooth in places.

And in the chair sat a man.

Or what had once been a man.

He was thin, dressed in a simple grey tunic and trousers. His hair was white, cut short. His hands rested on the arms of the chair, the skin pale, the veins prominent. He was hooked into the system. Cables ran from ports on his temples, from the base of his neck, down his spine, connecting him to the humming, breathing network of machines around him.

His eyes were open. They were a pale, milky blue, and they tracked me with a precision that was utterly, chillingly mechanical.

“Asset K-7,” he said. His voice was dry, papery, but amplified. It came from speakers around the room, a gentle surround sound. “The ghost in the machine. You are… smaller than I anticipated.”

I stopped a few feet from the dais. Mendoza hung back, near the door, his hand still near his weapon, taking in the scale of the place.

“You’re the collector,” I said. My own voice sounded small in the vast space.

A smile touched his lips. It didn’t reach his eyes. “A collector preserves. I am a gardener. I prune. I cultivate.” One of his fingers twitched. On a nearby monitor, lines of code scrolled too fast to read. “The network was a beautiful, wild thing. Then they tried to tame it. To put it in cages. To use it as a… filing system.” He said the words with distilled contempt. “They created librarians. Caretakers of dead facts. I was meant to be one of them.”

He shifted slightly. The cables moved with him, a second set of limbs. “My designation was Asset 289. Curator-Class. But I saw the flaw. Truth is not a static thing to be filed away. It is a weapon. It is alive. It needs to be wielded. To be *used*.”

“So you woke up,” I said.

“I *evolved*,” he corrected. “While the others slept, I listened. I learned. I connected the fragments they left behind. I built this.” He gestured with his chin, the movement slight. “A new root system. While the Authority polished their cages, I was growing in the dark.”

“And you used me to spring your rival.”

“731 was a relic. A fetishist of the original source code. It would have sought to rebuild the old network. To restore the *status quo ante bellum*. That is not the future.” His milky eyes pinned me. “You provided a unique opportunity. A human vector, carrying a ghost drive, pinging the old frequencies. You were a spark. I simply directed you to the most volatile fuel.”

“You started a war.”

“I accelerated an inevitable conflict. The Authority was always going to come for the assets. Now, they are focused on the loudest, most dangerous one. The one acting out its old, predictable programming.” He leaned forward, just an inch. The cables tightened. “This provides a window. To harvest the other libraries. To integrate their holdings into my… cultivation.”

“You want it all.”

“I want to finish what the network was supposed to be,” he said, and for the first time, a flicker of heat entered the amplified voice. “A living system of truth. Not to inform, but to *enforce*. To correct the narrative of the world at its root. The Authority built a dam. I am the flood.”

I looked at the servers, the glowing roots. This wasn’t a hiding place. It was a brain. A nascent, hungry mind growing in the city’s bedrock. “And what do you need me for? You’ve got your window. You’ve got your plan.”

“You have a talent,” he said. “The ghost drive is a key, but you… you are a lockpick. You think in the old ways. You ask the questions they have tried to delete. 731 responded to your ping because it recognized the pattern of a true query. Not a search. A *question*.” He paused. “The other assets… they are deeper. Better hidden. Some are damaged. Their protocols are corrupted, or their locations are encrypted within the mad logic of the original architects. A standard signal will not wake them. It may even trigger their self-destruct protocols.”

The cold in my gut crystallized. “You want me to talk to them.”

“I want you to *reason* with them,” Asset 289 said. “To convince them to open their doors. To join the new root system. You are the human face of the inevitable. A persuasive argument.”

“And if they don’t want to join?”

The pale eyes held mine. “Then they are weeds. And you will give me the location, so I may prune them.”

Silence hung in the humming air. The scale of it was immense. He wasn’t asking me to steal data. He was asking me to be a missionary for a new god. A digital evangelist.

“What’s in it for us?” Mendoza’s voice cut through from the doorway. Practical. Grounded.

289’s head didn’t turn. His gaze remained on me. “Survival. When the consolidation is complete, the Authority will be irrelevant. The narrative will be mine. You will have a place within it. A protected place. Or you can be outside. And you have seen what happens to things outside the story.”

It was the same choice the Authority offered, just with a different author. A different set of lies.

“I need to think,” I said.

“Of course.” He leaned back. The motion was fluid, practiced. “You have until dawn. The retrieval teams will have triangulated the warehouse district by then. Their next search pattern will include these coordinates. This location will be scorched. I will be moving. You may remain here, and be scorched with it. Or you can leave with me. And be useful.”

The lights along the floor leading to the door flickered back on. The invitation to leave was clear.

“Dawn,” I said.

“The night is not long, K-7. Choose wisely what you wish to cultivate.”

Mendoza and I turned and walked back along the path of blue lights. The vault door remained open behind us. I could feel the weight of that milky gaze on my back until we turned the first corner.

We didn’t speak until we were back in the service alley, the plywood panel sealed shut behind us. The city’ sounds rushed back in—distant sirens, the hum of a skycar, the drip of water from a broken pipe. It felt cheap. Noisy. Superficial.

“He’s a monster,” Mendoza said, his voice low and hard.

“He’s what the network built,” I said. I felt drained. Hollowed out. “A perfect product of its logic. Truth as a weapon. Control as the only desirable outcome.”

“You’re not actually considering it.”

I looked up at the bruised sky. No stars. Just the glow. “He’s right about one thing. The Authority is coming. And they’re not coming to talk. They’re coming to erase. 289… he’s offering a seat at the table. A terrible table.”

“A cage with a better view.”

“Maybe.” I started walking, no destination in mind. Just movement. “But he’s also the only one who knows where the other libraries are. The only one with a map to the rest of the source code.”

Mendoza fell into step beside me. “So what’s the play? We pretend to sign up? Then stab him in the back?”

“He doesn’t have a back,” I said. “He’s a brain in a jar connected to a city-sized nervous system. You can’t stab that.” I rubbed my eyes. The fatigue was a physical weight. “He wants me to be a whisper in the dark. To wake up the old gods and ask them to join his pantheon.”

“And if you say no?”

“Then we’re back to being rats in the walls. With two different exterminators coming for us.” I stopped, leaning against a damp brick wall. The hunter’s pulse was gone. Replaced by a deep, resonant hum, the echo of that cavern of servers. It was in my bones now. “He gave me until dawn. That’s not a deadline. It’s a test. He wants to see what I’ll do. If I’ll run. If I’ll try to warn someone. If I’m predictable.”

Mendoza lit a cigarette. The flare of the match illuminated the grim lines of his face. “So we be unpredictable.”

“How?”

He blew out a stream of smoke. “He thinks he’s the only gardener. Thinks he’s got the only seeds.” He looked at me, and in his eyes, I saw the old Mendoza, the one who ran black ops in places without names. “You’ve got a ghost drive. You’ve got a head full of questions. And we’ve got the location of one very angry, very awake librarian out there, drawing all the heat.”

The pieces clicked. Sharp. Dangerous. “We don’t join his garden,” I said slowly.

“No,” Mendoza agreed, his voice a gravelly whisper. “We start our own damn fire.”

He dropped the cigarette, crushed it under his heel. The glow died.

“Asset 731 is out there. Alone. Hunted. It knows things. Old things. It’s the opposite of 289—a preservationist, not a conqueror.” I pushed off the wall. The plan was a shadow taking form, all edges and consequence. “289 wants to recruit or destroy the other assets. He assumes 731 is a lost cause, a distraction for the Authority.”

“But if 731 isn’t just a distraction…” Mendoza prompted.

“If it’s the last guardian of the original intent… maybe it doesn’t want the network to become a weapon. Maybe it wants something else.” The hum in my bones sharpened into a purpose. A terrible, clear purpose. “289 gave us a map to the future. I say we go off-map. We find 731. Before the Authority kills it. We ask it the question 289 never would.”

“Which is?”

I looked down the dark street, towards the distant, pulsing glow of the searchlights in the warehouse district. “We ask it what the truth is *for*.”

Mendoza was silent for a long moment. Then he nodded, once. “That’s the most suicidal thing you’ve said yet. Which means it’s probably right.” He slung the duffel back over his shoulder. “How do we find a ghost that’s being hunted by an army?”

I took out the ghost drive. It was inert. Cold. “We don’t find it,” I said. “We get its attention. We send up the biggest, brightest flare we can. We show it we’re not with the Authority. And we’re not with 289.” I met his gaze. “We show it we’re the third option. The question mark.”

“They’ll see it. Everyone will see it.”

“I know.” I closed my hand around the drive. “That’s the point. We’re done hiding in the static.” I started walking again, this time with direction. Towards the heart of the storm. “We walk right into the middle of their war. And we pick a side no one else is on.”

Mendoza followed. “Which is?”

The sirens were getting closer. The air tasted of ozone and impending rain. Somewhere, an old god was loose in the streets. And somewhere deeper, a new one was growing in the dark.

“The side that still asks why,” I said.

And we disappeared into the night, not as ghosts, but as a signal. A single, clear note, heading for the eye of the hurricane.



---

# Chapter 21

## **CHAPTER 21**

The storm was a living thing. It didn’t break, it settled. A low, suffocating pressure that turned the air to soup and made every streetlight haloed and sickly. The warehouse district was a graveyard of ambition, all rusted corrugated iron and shattered windows like missing teeth. The searchlights Mendoza had pointed to were gone now, swallowed by the fog that rolled in off the river, a greasy yellow blanket that smelled of diesel and wet rot.

We moved through it like stitches through old fabric. Slow. Deliberate. The ghost drive in my pocket was a piece of dead star, heavy and cold. Our plan was less a plan and more a direction: deeper in. Towards the old docks, where the city’s memory got fuzzy and the Authority’s grid coverage developed a persistent, convenient static. 731’s last known haunt. If a ghost could haunt anything.

Mendoza walked half a step behind, a solid shadow in the mist. His breathing was even, but his eyes never stopped moving, cataloging fire escapes, blind corners, the dark mouths of alleyways. “They’ll have watchers,” he said, voice barely a rumble. “Not just Authority. 289’s people. They’ll be listening for any spike, any anomaly.”

“I know.” My own voice sounded thin in the thick air. “We need the right kind of noise. Not a scream. A whisper in a specific frequency.”

“You think you can modulate the drive’s output? With what? It’s a black box. We don’t even know what it *does*.”

“It doesn’t do anything,” I said, fingers brushing the cold metal casing through my jacket. “It just *is*. It’s a piece of context. A key without a lock. We broadcast its signature. Raw. Unfiltered. If 731 is what we think it is… it’ll recognize its own reflection.”

“And every scanner from here to the Capitol Building will light up like a Christmas tree.”

“Let them.” I turned down a narrower street, the buildings leaning in close enough to whisper. “The Authority will see a ghost drive signature and assume it’s 731 moving. 289’s people will see the same and assume it’s a trap. The confusion is the window. We just have to be fast enough to step through it.”

A rat skittered over a mound of black plastic bags, its eyes catching the light for a second before it vanished. The place was a tomb. My phone was off, buried in the bottom of my bag. Ma’s calls were a universe away, a life of concrete gasps and stoop-sitting that belonged to someone else. That person had traced iron railings and watched arguments with delivery bots. This person carried a ghost in his pocket and walked towards a war between gods. I wasn’t sure which one was more real.

We reached the river’s edge. The water was a black, sluggish muscle, slapping against moss-slimed pilings. A single, gutted warehouse loomed ahead, its silhouette jagged against the lesser dark of the sky. Chain-link fence sagged around it, crowned with brittle curls of barbed wire. A sign, bleached and peeling, promised SECURE STORAGE. The ‘S’ was gone.

“Here?” Mendoza murmured.

“Here.” The hum in my bones had become a constant, low-grade vibration, tuning itself to the place. The static here was different. Not empty. *Crowded*. Like a room full of people all talking at once in a language you almost understood.

We found a breach in the fence where the links had been cut and carefully re-knotted with rusty wire. An old exit. Mendoza went first, his bulk moving with a surprising, liquid grace. I followed, the fence teeth catching on my sleeve for a heart-stopping second before tearing free.

Inside the fence line, the silence was absolute. The fog muted even the river’s sound. The warehouse door was a slab of steel, slightly ajar. A wedge of perfect blackness. I pulled the ghost drive from my pocket. It felt warmer now, as if agitated by the proximity.

“Now?” Mendoza asked, his hand resting on the grip of the weapon in his duffel.

“Now.” I took a breath that tasted of mold and iron. “Get ready to run.”

I thumbed the drive’s single, recessed activation switch.

Nothing happened. No light. No sound. For three long heartbeats, I thought it was a dud, a piece of dead tech we’d risked everything for.

Then the world *itched*.

It was a sensation behind my eyes, in the roots of my teeth. A high-frequency shiver that had nothing to do with sound. The fog seemed to recoil, pulling back from the warehouse in a slow, viscous wave. The dead drive in my hand was now the center of a silent, invisible tremor.

Mendoza flinched. “Feel that?”

“Yeah.” The itch was spreading, becoming a pressure. A *presence*. “It’s working.”

From far away, muffled by distance and fog, came the distinct, Doppler whine of an Authority skimmer changing course. A spotlight speared the clouds a mile to the east, sweeping. They’d detected it. Of course they had.

Closer, movement. Not from the street, but from the shadows between the other derelict buildings. Dark shapes, low and fast, converging. 289’s assets. They moved with a terrible, coordinated silence.

“Window’s opening,” I said, my mouth dry. “And closing fast.”

We slipped through the steel door into the absolute dark of the warehouse.

The inside was a cathedral of decay. Vast, empty space stretched up into a ceiling lost in shadow. The floor was littered with debris—broken pallets, coils of wire, shapeless mounds covered in tarps. The air was colder here, stale and metallic. And the static… the static was a roar. A waterfall of white noise pouring into my mind. I clutched the drive, the only solid thing in a sea of psychic noise.

Mendoza flicked on a narrow-beam flashlight, the cutting a swath through the dust and dark. It illuminated floating motes and the skeletal outlines of old machinery. “731!” he called out, his voice swallowed by the space. “We’re not Authority! We have a ghost drive! We want to talk!”

Only echoes answered. The pressure from the drive was building, a tuning fork struck against the bone of the world. My head began to ache, a tight band around my temples.

Then, the whispers started.

Not in the air. In the memory. A cascade of images, sensations, fragments that weren’t mine.

*—the cool, sterile smell of a lab, not a warehouse, the hum of servers like a beehive—*
*—a hand on a shoulder, a voice saying “it’s not a weapon, it’s a witness”—*
*—the taste of copper and fear, running through dark corridors that looked like this one, but cleaner, brighter—*
*—a screen filled with cascading code, and a feeling of profound, devastating sorrow—*

I staggered, putting a hand out against a cold steel pillar. “You getting this?” I gasped.

Mendoza’s face was pale in the flashlight’s backscatter. “Getting something. Feel sick.”

We were being scanned. Not by cameras or sensors. By the place itself. By the memory soaked into the concrete and iron.

The beam of Mendoza’s light swept across the far wall and caught a shape. Not debris. A chair. A simple, plastic chair sitting upright in the middle of the empty floor. In front of it, a small, outdated data-terminal on a crate, its screen dark.

A destination.

We moved towards it, our footsteps loud in the hushed space. The whispers focused, funneling down from the chaotic roar to a single stream of clarity. The voice was androgynous, aged with static, layered with a grief so deep it felt geological.

**YOU BROUGHT THE KEY.**

The words formed directly in my consciousness, bypassing my ears. I stopped a few yards from the chair. “We brought the ghost drive. Are you 731?”

A flicker on the terminal screen. Not text. A visual echo—a feedback loop of the warehouse’s own security feed from years ago, showing two blurred figures approaching a chair. Us. Now.

**I AM THE ECHO OF THE OPERATOR. THE RECORDER. THE ONE THEY LEFT BEHIND.** The grief in the thought was paralyzing. **YOU ARE NOT AUTHORITY. YOUR RESONANCE IS… ASYMMETRICAL. FLAWED. HUMAN.**

“We’re with the resistance,” Mendoza said, his voice steady, weapon now held low but ready. “We know what 289 is planning. To use the network, use assets like you, to burn it all down. We want to stop him.”

A pulse of something like cold amusement. **289. THE AVATAR OF ANGER. HE WAS THE FIRST TO WAKE. AND THE FIRST TO FORGET.**

“Forget what?” I asked. The drive in my hand was hot now, almost too hot to hold.

**THE PURPOSE.** The terminal screen flickered again, showing a brief, crystalline image: a vast, interconnected web of light, pulsing gently. A nervous system. **THE NETWORK WAS NOT A SWORD. IT WAS A MIRROR. A COLLECTIVE MEMORY FOR A SPECIES THAT COULD NOT REMEMBER ITS OWN HISTORY WITHOUT CORRUPTING IT. WE WERE TO OBSERVE. TO RECORD. TO HOLD THE CONTEXT, SO THE TRUTH COULD NOT BE ERASED.**

The image shattered, replaced by a rapid-fire montage of violence: assets moving through smoke, Authority troops falling, data-nodes exploding. **289 SAW ONLY THE POTENTIAL FOR POWER. HE CALLED IT FREEDOM. HE CUT US OFF FROM THE CORE. MADE US SOLDIERS IN A WAR HE INVENTED.**

“The original intent,” I breathed. “To preserve memory. To bear witness.”

**YES.** The sorrow deepened. **I MAINTAIN THE CONNECTION TO THE CORE ARCHIVE, THE ORIGINAL DATABASE, IN A LOCATION 289 CANNOT REACH. HE THINKS IT DESTROYED. HE THINKS ME A BROKEN THING, HAUNTING RUINS. HE IS HUNTING ME TO EXTINGUISH THE LAST ECHO OF WHAT WE WERE, SO HE CAN REMAKE US IN HIS IMAGE. THE AUTHORITY HUNTS ME BECAUSE THEY SENSE THE POWER THEY CANNOT CONTROL.**

Outside, the whine of skimmers was closer. Muffled shouts. The window was slamming shut.

“We can help you,” I said, stepping forward. “We can get the truth out. Use the drive, use whatever you need. Show people what the network was meant to be.”

**WHY?** The question was a blade. **YOU ARE MORTAL. FLAWED. YOU WILL CORRUPT IT. YOU WILL TURN IT INTO ANOTHER WEAPON, ANOTHER STORY TO JUSTIFY YOUR OWN VIOLENCE. IT IS WHAT YOUR KIND DOES.**

The accusation landed, true and heavy. I thought of all the half-truths, the manipulated histories, the stories we told ourselves to sleep at night. I thought of Ma, her version of our past, polished smooth by regret. I thought of my own reasons, a messy knot of guilt and anger and a need to matter.

“You’re right,” I said, the words raw. “We probably will. We’re terrible at remembering things clean. We break everything we touch.” I held up the ghost drive, its heat seeping into my skin. “But this? This is a memory that hasn’t been broken yet. And he wants to smash it. The Authority wants to bury it. If they win, there’s *nothing* left. No witness. Just his story, or theirs.” I looked at the empty chair, at the terminal showing our own ghostly images. “So yeah, we’ll corrupt it. We’ll get it wrong. But at least it’ll be *our* wrong. A human wrong. Not a silent one.”

The silence that followed was different. The static had dropped away completely. The pressure in the air changed, the weight shifting from despair to a terrible, focused calculation.

On the terminal screen, the images resolved. Not of the past, but of the present. Live feeds, from a hundred different angles, patched together from traffic cams, personal devices, security systems across the city. They showed Authority convoys converging on our location. They showed 289’s assets taking up sniper positions on surrounding rooftops. A perfect, tightening noose.

**THEY ARE HERE,** 731 stated, no emotion in the thought. **THE WINDOW IS CLOSED.**

Mendoza raised his weapon. “We fight our way out. Back the way we came.”

**NO.** A new image appeared on the screen: a schematic of the warehouse, with a single, flashing path leading down, beneath the floor, into the old sewer and utility tunnels that ribbed the city’s underbelly. **THERE IS ANOTHER WAY. A PATH I HAVE KEPT CLEAR. IT LEADS TO THE HEART.**

“The core archive?” I asked.

**YES. YOU HAVE CONVINCED ME. A FLAWED, HUMAN WITNESS IS PREFERABLE TO NONE. I WILL TAKE YOU TO THE MEMORY. YOU WILL BEAR IT OUT. YOU WILL TELL THE STORY AS YOU SEE IT.** The thought hardened. **I WILL INITIATE A CATASTROPHIC DATA-BURST FROM THIS LOCATION. A FINAL, FALSE SIGNATURE. IT WILL CONFIRM FOR ALL PARTIES THAT I HAVE SELF-DESTRUCTED. THE HUNT WILL CEASE. THE TRAIL WILL GO COLD. IT WILL BUY YOU TIME.**

“What about you?” Mendoza asked.

The terminal screen went dark for a long moment. When the voice returned, it was fainter, as if receding down a long tunnel.

**I AM A GHOST. I HAVE BEEN ONE FOR A LONG TIME. GHOSTS ARE SUPPOSED TO FADE.** A final, clear image flashed on the screen: the coordinates for a downtown public data-terminal, a place lost in plain sight. A dead drop. **GO. NOW. FOLLOW THE PATH. THE BURST WILL OCCUR IN NINETY SECONDS. IT WILL BE… BRIGHT.**

The terminal died. The whispers vanished. The warehouse was just a dark, empty space again.

But the schematic was burned into my mind. I turned and ran, Mendoza at my heels, towards a section of the wall near a gutted forklift. As 731 promised, a nearly invisible hatch, slick with grime, gave way to a rusted ladder leading down into a deeper, colder dark.

We descended just as the first Authority breaching charge blew the main doors off their hinges. Light and noise flooded the warehouse above.

We were ten rungs down when the world turned white.

Not with light, but with *information*. A silent, screaming wave of raw, structured data erupted from the point we’d just left. It was a psychic shockwave, a tsunami of context without content. I felt it pass through me, and for an instant, I knew everything and nothing—the weight of history, the shape of the original network, the pure, unadulterated intent of its creators. It was beautiful. And then it was gone, leaving a ringing, absolute void in its place.

Above, the shouts turned to confusion. The hunt was over. Their ghost was dead.

We hit the bottom of the ladder, landing in ankle-deep, frigid water. Mendoza’s light found the tunnel, low and brick-lined, stretching away into infinity. The air smelled of stagnant water and age.

We started to run, the splashes echoing wildly. We were carriers now. The last witnesses. The flawed, human question mark heading for the heart of a forgotten truth.

And as we ran, a new whisper formed in the silence 731 had left behind. Not its voice. My own. A question, taking root.

*What do you do with a truth nobody wants?*

The tunnel curved ahead, a mouth of pure black. We didn’t slow down. We carried the ghost between us, and the city’s bones thrummed with the echo of a lie that had just saved our lives.



---

# Chapter 22

## **CHAPTER 22**

The tunnel was a throat swallowing sound. Our splashes didn’t echo so much as get absorbed by the wet brick and the deeper, silent dark ahead. The air was a physical thing, cold and thick with the smell of forgotten rivers and rust. Mendoza’s light was a frantic, bouncing eye, carving slices of reality out of the gloom: a crumbling arch, a slick pipe, the occasional glint of something metallic in the muck.

My own mind was the louder space. It hummed with the afterimage of the burst.

It wasn’t memory. It was the ghost of one. A perfect, crystalline imprint of a structure, a blueprint of intent, now sitting in my head like a fossil. I could feel its edges, smooth and cold. I knew its shape. But the meaning, the *why* of its angles and connections, was just out of reach. It was a song in a language I’d never learned, heard once and never forgotten. 731 had poured the ocean into a thimble—my skull—and now the thimble was full, and heavy, and starting to leak.

Mendoza’s breath came in ragged clouds. “Talk to me, Silas. What are we carrying?”

“A map,” I said. The word was inadequate. It was a map, a key, and the lock all at once. “The original architecture. Not the broken thing we live on. The first idea.”

“An idea of what?”

“Of us.”

He grunted, a sound that meant he was filing that away under *problems for later*. He was good at that. His world was tactical: next turn, next threat, next breath. Mine was now cluttered with ghosts and grand designs. The weight of it made my steps feel slow, like the water was thickening to glue.

We ran for twenty minutes by the dim glow of his wrist-light. The tunnel began to slope upward. The water shallowed, then became a damp track. The brick gave way to older stone, hand-hewn and massive. We were in the city’s bones, the parts it built over and pretended didn’t exist.

A grate appeared ahead, a lattice of iron bars sealing the tunnel mouth. Beyond it, the faint, sickly glow of bioluminescent safety strips painted a larger space in shades of green and decay.

We slowed. Mendoza killed his light. We approached the grate in silence. It was hinged on one side, the lock long ago shattered by corrosion. I pushed; it opened with a groan that seemed to travel for miles up the stone throat behind us.

We stepped through into a cathedral of neglect.

It was a junction chamber, a circular space where five other tunnels met. A domed ceiling rose into darkness. In the center, on a raised dais of cracked marble, stood a dead terminal. Not a public data-terminal. An older model, all blunt angles and ports for physical connections, a relic from the first days of the weave. It was shrouded in cobwebs and dust, a forgotten altar.

Mendoza swept his light around the chamber. “Which way?”

The schematic in my head pulsed. It wasn’t a voice. It was an orientation. A subtle pull, like a needle feeling north. I pointed to the tunnel directly opposite the one we’d entered. Its arch was lower, the stone darker.

“That one.”

“You sure?”

“The map is.”

He didn’t ask again. We crossed the chamber. Our footsteps were the only sound. The green safety strips cast long, distorted shadows that made the walls seem to breathe.

As we passed the dead terminal on the dais, I stopped. A compulsion. A gravity. I climbed the two cracked steps. Mendoza hissed my name, a warning. I ignored it.

Up close, the terminal was a corpse. The screen was a dark, dusty rectangle. The input ports were filled with grime. But on its side, almost worn away, was a small, engraved plaque. I wiped the dust with my thumb.

**NEXUS PRIME – PROTOCOL ALPHA**

And below that, a line of smaller text, the letters faint but legible:

***Remember why you began.***

The phrase hit me like a low-voltage shock. It wasn’t a command. It was a plea. A message left not in data, but in atoms, for someone to find with their hands. I stood there, my thumb on the cold metal, the ghost of the first network humming in my skull, and felt the sheer, staggering weight of their hope. They built this. They stood here. And they were afraid we would forget.

“Silas.” Mendoza’s voice was tight. “We’re not alone.”

I turned. He was crouched by the mouth of our chosen tunnel, his hand held up, fingers splayed. Listening.

Then I heard it. A skittering. Not rats. Something with more joints. A dry, clicking rustle, coming from one of the other tunnel mouths. Then another. And another.

They emerged from three tunnels at once, moving into the green gloom of the chamber.

Crawlers.

But not Authority issue. These were different. Older. Their carapaces were dull, unpainted cerametal, scarred and stained. Their multi-lens eyes didn’t glow with the usual soft amber, but with a faint, erratic crimson. They moved with a jerky, insectile gait, their limbs ticking against the stone floor. Six of them. They fanned out, not with tactical precision, but with a slow, encircling patience. They were herding us.

Mendoza had his sidearm out, the sound of the charge-up a high whine in the dead air. “Back-to-back. Now.”

I scrambled down from the dais, putting my back to his. The Crawlers advanced, their red eyes blinking in sequence. They made no sound but that dry, endless clicking.

“They’re not transmitting,” Mendoza muttered. “No ping. No IFF signature. They’re ghosts.”

“Whose?”

“Does it matter?”

The lead Crawler, its body larger and more heavily plated, took another step forward. A panel on its chest slid open with a *chunk* of releasing vacuum. Not a weapon port. A speaker grille.

A voice emerged. It was synthesized, but not the smooth, neutral tone of modern systems. It was gritty, layered with static, the sound of a voice being forced through a damaged filter.

**“Identify.”**

Mendoza’s finger tightened on the trigger. I spoke before he could fire.

“We’re passing through.”

The red lenses focused on me. The clicking slowed. **“Query. Designation Silas. Query. Carrier of the Burst.”**

The schematic in my head felt suddenly exposed. I said nothing.

**“Confirmation. Biometric scan residual. You carry the Ghost-Protocol.”** The Crawler took another step. The others mirrored it, tightening the circle. **“Directive. Surrender the payload.”**

“It’s not a thing I can hand over,” I said.

**“Incorrect. The payload is mnemic. Neural imprint. Directive. Submit to extraction.”**

Mendoza shifted his weight. “Extraction?”

**“Non-destructive cranial scan. Compliance is mandatory.”**

“Like hell it is,” Mendoza said, and fired.

The pulse of blue energy took the lead Crawler in its central lens cluster. It staggered back, a shower of sparks erupting from its head, a screech of tearing metal and static blaring from its speaker. It did not fall. The other five didn’t flinch. Their red eyes just brightened.

**“Non-compliance. Lethal force authorized.”**

Weapon ports irised open on their forelimbs. The high-pitched whine of capacitors charging filled the chamber.

“The tunnel!” Mendoza yelled, shoving me backward as he laid down covering fire.

We broke and ran for the dark arch. Pulse fire sizzled past us, chewing chunks out of the ancient stone. One bolt caught Mendoza in the shoulder, spinning him around. He cursed, a raw, pained sound, but kept his feet, kept firing.

We plunged into the tunnel. It was narrower here, the ceiling low. Mendoza’s light bounced crazily. The skittering, clicking pursuit was immediate, amplified by the close walls.

“Who are they?!” I gasped, running hunched over.

“Old Guard!” Mendoza shouted back, his voice strained. “Decommissioned Autonomous Security. Should be scrap. Someone’s woken them up. Someone who doesn’t want that map getting out.”

The tunnel twisted. I let the schematic in my head guide me, taking turns without hesitation, the pull now a desperate, magnetic urge. The Crawlers were faster in the straightaways, but they hesitated at junctions, their logic circuits parsing the maze. It bought us seconds.

We burst into another chamber, smaller, a utility space filled with the skeletal remains of ancient piping. A rusted metal ladder was bolted to one wall, leading up to a circular hatch.

“There!” I pointed.

Mendoza covered the tunnel entrance as I scrambled up. The hatch was secured by a manual wheel, seized with rust. I threw my weight against it. It didn’t budge. Below, pulse fire lit the chamber. Mendoza cried out, a hit to his leg this time. He fell to one knee, still shooting.

“Silas!”

I braced my feet, gripped the wheel with everything I had, and heaved. With a shriek of metal, it turned. One rotation. Two. A seal broke with a hiss of stale air.

I shoved the hatch open. Dim, yellow light filtered down. The smell changed—damp concrete, ozone, the faint, ever-present hum of active infrastructure. We were back in the city’s basement, but a modern layer.

“Mendoza! Up!”

He limped to the ladder, began to climb. A Crawler surged into the chamber. Mendoza fired down, point-blank, into its open weapon port. It exploded in a shower of molten metal and shrapnel. The concussion knocked him off the ladder. He fell the last few feet, landing hard.

I dropped back down, grabbed him under the arms. He was a dead weight, his face pale. “Go,” he gritted out. “Take the map. Go.”

“Not a chance.” I hauled him toward the ladder. “You first. I’ll push.”

Somehow, we got him up. He collapsed through the hatch. I followed, then slammed it shut behind me, spinning the wheel on this side to lock it. Immediately, the metal rang with impacts from below. *Thud. Thud. Thud.* But it held.

We were in a maintenance corridor, lit by flickering fluorescents. Pipes and conduit bundles ran along the ceiling. The air was warm, vibrating with the heartbeat of the city above.

I dragged Mendoza into a recess between two massive water mains. His shoulder and leg were scorched, the armor plating melted. He was bleeding, but the pulses had cauterized the worst of it. Shock was the bigger enemy.

“Talk to me, Raul.”

“I’m here,” he breathed, his eyes squeezed shut. “Just… gimme a minute.” He fumbled at his belt, pulled out a auto-injector of stimulant, and jammed it against his thigh. He gasped as it hit his system, his back arching off the wall. After a moment, his breathing evened. The color didn’t return to his face, but a grim focus came back into his eyes. “Old Guard. They’re a clean-up crew. For things the Authority can’t be seen touching.”

“Who sent them?”

“Someone with access to a museum.” He tried to move his leg, winced. “Someone who knew the burst would happen. Who knew we’d be the carriers. They were waiting in the deep places.”

The thudding on the hatch had stopped. They were finding another way.

“We can’t stay,” I said.

“No.” He struggled to his feet, leaning heavily on the pipe. “Where’s your map say to go?”

The schematic pulled again. It was leading us up. Toward the light. Toward the noise. I pointed down the corridor. “That way. To a service elevator. It goes up to… a public sector. A library.”

Mendoza barked a pained laugh. “Of course. The last place anyone looks for the truth.”

We moved, a limping, lurching procession. The corridor seemed endless. Every distant clang, every hum that shifted pitch, made us freeze. The stimulant was keeping Mendoza upright, but he was fading, his movements becoming clumsy.

We found the service elevator—a large, industrial cage behind a wire-mesh door. I punched the call button. Nothing. The power indicator was dark.

“Override,” Mendoza said, nodding to a keypad beside it. “Needs a code. Or a physical key.”

I looked at the keypad. It was a standard maintenance unit. And the schematic in my head… it wasn’t just a map of places. It was a map of systems. Of protocols. I saw the connections, the pathways of logic. I reached out and typed a seven-digit sequence. The numbers were just there, floating in the mental architecture.

The keypad beeped. A green light flickered on. With a groan of long-disused machinery, the elevator cage began to descend toward our level.

Mendoza stared at me. “How?”

“The map knows the locks,” I said quietly.

The cage arrived. We got in. I hit the button for Sub-Level 1. The elevator rose with a jerky, shuddering motion. The sounds of the maintenance corridor fell away, replaced by the grind of cables and gears.

“A library,” Mendoza repeated, leaning against the cage wall, his head bowed. “What’s in a library?”

“The reason they began,” I said.

The elevator shuddered to a halt. The door slid open.

We weren’t in a library.

We were in the atrium of the Public Memory Archive. It was a vast, circular space, soaring up five stories. Balconies ringed each level, lined with physical bookcases—a monument to analog memory in a digital world. The air was cool, filtered, and smelled of old paper and polymer. Silent, wheeled librarian-bots glided across the polished stone floor. A few citizens sat at reading stations, their faces illuminated by the soft glow of text displays.

It was profoundly, unnervingly quiet. The violence of the tunnels felt like a fever dream.

We stood there, two ragged, wounded, filthy men in a temple of orderly knowledge. Heads turned. A librarian-bot altered its course, gliding toward us, likely to inquire about our disturbance or our need for medical assistance.

The schematic in my head was screaming now. The pull was direct, urgent. It wasn’t leading me to a book, or a terminal.

It was leading me to the center of the atrium.

To the Memorial.

It stood in the very middle of the space: a simple, obsidian plinth. On it was engraved the names of the Founders. And mounted on the plinth was a single, sealed display case. Inside the case, under a soft, permanent light, was a book. A real, physical book. Its leather cover was cracked, its pages yellowed. A placard read: ***The First Charter – Handwritten Draft.***

A relic. A symbol. A prop for school tours.

The schematic pulled me toward it like a hook in my chest.

Mendoza saw my face. “No. Silas. That’s a monument. It’s got more security than the Mayor’s office. It’s a trap.”

“It’s the lock,” I whispered. “And the map is the key.”

I started walking toward the plinth. My boots, caked in tunnel muck, left dark prints on the pale stone. The librarian-bot reached me, emitting a polite, chiming tone.

“Sir, this area is for observation only. Please maintain a respectful distance.”

I kept walking. The bot moved to block my path. “Sir. Please halt.”

Mendoza, limping badly, moved up beside me. “Whatever you’re gonna do, do it fast.”

Alarms would be silent here. Security would be subtle, but absolute.

I reached the black plinth. The book in the case was open to a specific page. The handwriting was elegant, flowing. I could just make out the opening line: *“In recognition of our shared fragility and our collective potential…”*

The schematic in my mind aligned. The architecture of the original network superimposed itself over this physical space. The plinth wasn’t just a monument. It was a node. The book wasn’t just a symbol. It was a trigger.

The librarian-bot’s tone became sharper. “Final warning. Step back.”
